This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission, which visited
Zaire in November 1984, consisting of A. Ceyhan (Power Engineer, Mission
Leader), J. V. Gontier (Financial Analyst, Consultant) and S. Kalinowski
(Training  Expert, Consultant).   The mission also drew largely on the
findings of Mr. Dubois (Power Economist, Consultant) of the Energy
Assessment Mission which visited Zaire -during the same period.
Institutional and financial analyses were carried out by K. Mikitin and
E. Bundi; the energy sector chapter was written by M. Dyson; and the
economic analysis was reviewed by G. Schramm. Final version of the report
is based on the findings of an updating mission, consisting of A. Ceyhan
and K. Mikitin, which visited Zaire in March 1986.

ZAIRE
SECOND POIWER PROJECT
Loan and Project Summary
Borrower:                   The Government of the Republic of Zaire
Amount:                     IDA Credit SDR 37.3 million (US$37 million
equivalent)
Terms:                      Standard
Cofinancing:                UNDP grant of US$600,000 equivalent.
Beneficiary:                Societe Nationale d'Electricit6 (SNEL)
Onlending Terms:            The Government would onlend SDR 37.2 million
(US$36.9 million equivalent) from the proceeds
of the Credits to ShEL at 8.5 percent interest
with a 20 year repayment period, including five
years' grace.   SNEL will  bear the foreign
exchange risk. UNDP funds would be on a grant
basis.
Project Description:        The project would seek to:
(a) ensure reliability of supply to the main
interconnected    networks,    by      (i)
rehabilitating the existing Ruzizi I plant
and the distribution networks of Bukavu,
Goma, Uvira and Kiliba; (ii) completing
the  rehabilitation  of  the  generation
stations and transmission and distribution
system in the Shaba Region; and (iii)
carrying out urgent maintenance works and
improving operation of the Inga hydropower
complex;
(b) make optimal use of the electric energy
within the Zaire (Kivu)-Rwanda-Burundi
interconnected system by increasing the
transmission and distribution capacity of
the electricity network in that portion of
the   Kivu   region   where   there   is
considerable suppressed demand;
(c) strengthen SNEL as an institution through
better   planning,    rational   pricing,
improved financial policies, and training
of its staff. An urban household energy
study to be supervised by SNEL on behalf
of the Government would determine the
substitutability   of   electricity   for
traditional household fuels.



Benefits and Risks:         The principal benefits of the proposed project
would result from continued use of some of
Zaire' s earlier major power investments and
from optimal use of power supplied through
Zairels interconnected networks. Completion of
the rebabilitation of the Shaba power plants
and transmission system and assistance to SNEL
in operating and maintaining the Inga I and
Inga II hydropower plants would improve
reliability of supply to the main electrified
areas and to the vital mining industry.
Pbysical risks of the project are mnlimal.  A
major risk lies in the Government's lack of
coherent policies for planning and pricing, but
by seeking advice through an Energy Sector
Assessment and committing itself to exchange
views with IDA on these subjects in the future,
the Government has demonstrated its intention
to improve in these areas.    SNEL's lack of
skilled manposer is also a risk, but this is
addressed by the provision of consultants and
training.

I. TH. ENERGY SECTOR
Background
1.01        Zaire is about 2.3 million square kilometers and is Africa's
third largest country.  The population of about 30.7 million is growing
overall at about 3% per year, but at about 7% in the cities where
approximately a third lives.   About three-quarters of the population
subsists on agriculture. Zaire's GNP per capita, estimated at about US$140
in 1984, is among the lowest in Africa.   Mining and mineral processing
account for a third of GDP and about two-thirds of the country' s export
earnings.
1.02      Zaire has abundant energy resources; particularly hydropower and
forests, but it also ,has some petroleum and coal, both of which are
produced in modest quantities.  Final energy consumption in 1983 totalled
about 8.6 million toe or about 280 kg of oil equivalent per capita.
Biomass energy sources (wood, charcoal, crop and animal residues) provided
for 86% of total energy consumption; about 80Z of which is used as
household energy (cooking, heating and lighting).   About 14% of total
energy consumption (or 39 kg of oil equivalent per capita in 1983) is made
up of petroleum (8%), power (4%) and coal and coke (2%). 1/
1.03      The energy balance for 1983 is given in Annex 1.1 and a summary
of estimated energy production and consumption for 1983 is provided in
Table 1.1.
Table 1.1
Estimated Energy Production and Consumption in 1983 a/

Source: Zaire: Issues and Options in the Energy Sector.
The main consumer of modern energy forms (petroleum, power and coal) in
Zaire is the mining industry, which accounts for about 39% followed by
transportation (36%) and manufacturing (9%). The remaining 16% is consumed
by agriculture, commerce and private households and the Government (Table
1.2).   Consumption of petroleum products and electricity is expected to
grow at a faster pace than fuelwood, but the latter will remain the
predominant energy source in the foreseeable future.

Energy Sector Assessment Program, May 1986 (Report No. 5837-ZR).

Energy Sector Organization and Planning
1.04        The DME (D6partement des mines et de 1'energie) has primary
responsibility for formulating and implementing energy sector policy and is
charged with technical supervision of the Societe Nationale d'Electricite
(SNEL - Chapter III) and the Entreprise Petroliere du Zaire (PetroZaire -
para. 1.06); no role has yet been defined for the Ministry with regard to
traditional household energy resources which are de facto being handled by
the Ministry of Environment, Nature Conservation and Tourism and its
agencies.  The Government has a broad involvement in the sector through
ownership in all energy enterprises (the main ones being SNEL and petroleum
exploration, refining, distribution and service companies) and ministries
responsible for pricing (Economy and Industry), investment review (Plan)
and forestry/fuelvood (Environment, Nature Conservation and Tourism).
Petrozaire holds a product processing contract with Societe Zaire-Italienne
de Raffinage (SOZIR - para. 1.07).  It also holds minority shares in the
semi-public petroleum products, distribution, marketing and service
companies (distribution: Zaire Fina, Zaire Mobil, Zaire Texaco and Zaire
Shell;  service: Zaire SEP).   The Department  of National Economy  and
Industry has authority over the prices of electricity and oil products.
The Department of Portfolio administers the Government's holdings in the
energy companies mentioned above.    The Ministry of Environment, Nature
Conservation and Tourism administers Zaire's forestry and fuelwood sector.
The National Reforestation Service (NRS) and the Center for the Adaptation
of Wood Energy Technology (CATEB) are other government units which are
related to the woodfuel sub-sector.



- 3 -
1.0c      The various energy entities have carried out their activities in
isolation from each other with no one of them having access to all the
information required to define sector policies or the capacity to implement
them. To address this problem, the Government created in 1981 a National
Energy Commission (NEC) under the DME and mandated it with coordinating the
work of all sector entities and defining a national energy policy. The NEC
is composed of officials from DME, the Department of National Economy and
Industry, REGIDESO (the water supply company) and the Service Presidentiel
d'Etudes (SeE). Lack of budgetary funds and guidance (an adviser financed
by the French Caisse Centrale de Cooperation Economique (CCCE) began his
two-year term in December 1985) have rendered the Commission ineffective
thus far. Definition of a technical support structure and a work program
for the NEC are under preparation and reallocation of extra budgetary funds
is proposed to launch the work of the NEC by Fall 1986.
1.06        PetroZaire was established in 1978 as an internal transport
agent. By 1984 it had acquired 5% share in the distribution of petroleum
products and had become the sole importer of petroleum products and crude.
According to its broad statutory powers, Petrozaire also has responsibility
for monitoring petroleum exploration activities; but up to now has carried
out little of its mandate in this area.
1.07      SOZIR, which is equally owned by AGIP (Italy) and the Government
of Zaire, operates Zaire's only refinery near Moanda/Banana, at the mouth
of the Zaire River. The refinery was built to process 750,000 tons/year of
imported crude but utilizacion has been consistently less.   All locally
produced crude is therefore exported and refined product needs met through
imports. Refinery operations ceased in 1984 because of a working capital
shortage among companies in the supply and distribution chain, and although
the liquidity situation has improved, a period of favorable world market
prices  for products  precluded  re-opening of  the  refinery.    A joint
Government, SOZIR and Bank position on the role of the refinery will be
developed through an IDA (PPF) financed study on the supply and
distribution system and is likely to orient SOZIR toward product unloading
and storage management for which it has facilities and expertise.
1.08       In relation to the hydropower resources it shares with Burundi
and Rwanda, Zaire belongs to two international institutions, Energie des
pays des grands lacs (EGL) and Societe  internationale d'electricite des
pays des grands lacs (SINELAC, para. 2.01).
1.09        One of the most important institutional deficiencies in the
en_rgy sector is the Government's neglect of the rational exploitation of
its biomass resources and tendency to concentrate primarily on the issues
of  petroleum resources and electric power.   In its capacity as  the
coordinating agent for various energy subsector interests, the t.EC would
give special emphasis to the problems of biomass energy.
Bank/IDA Participation in the Sector and Rationale for Involvement
1.10         The Shaba Power System Rehabilitation Project (CR 1224-ZR;
April 1, 1982; US$19 milli.n) was the Bank Group's first lending operation
in  the energy  sector.   The project is cofinanced with  the Belgian
Government and CCCE.   The purpose of the project, which consists  of



- 4 -
rehabilitation of the hydrogenerators and transmission system, maintenance
and technical assistance for the Shaba region, is to ensure an efficient
power supply to La general des carrieres et des mines (GECAMINES), the main
copper mining entity, whose operations are located in Shaba in the south of
the country. The project, though initially somewhat delayed, is proceeding
reasonably well.    Although some  important  institution building  e.g.,
management organization and training, and tariff issues are being addressed
through tiiis project, the physical and financial aspects are limited to the
Shaba Region.   During the rehabilitation works, the need for repair and
replacement of equipment surpassed the special contingency provided for
this purpose; additional materials, equipment and works are therefore
included  in  the  proposed  project  to  complete  the  rehabilitation
(para. 4.05).
1.11      With Burundi and Rwanda, Zaire is participating in the Ruzizi II
Hydroelectric Project (CRs 1419-BU, 1420-RW and 1421-ZR, US$45 million,
December 6, 1983). The project is cofinanced by the European Development
Fund (FED) and the Banque de DEveloppement Economique des Pays des Grands
Lacs  (BDEGL).    The project is a joint collaboration  formed  to take
advantage of economies of scale and to lower the costs of power supply to
Burundi, Rwanda and the Kivu region of eastern Zaire. The project consists
of a concrete gravity dam llm high, a power station (2x13 MW and space for
a third), switching station and 15 km of 110 kV transmission line, housing,
consulting engineering, institutional assistance to SINELAC, and a tariff
study.  Works started about one year late on this project and the power
plant is now expected to be operational by end-1988 or early-1989 (para.
2.17).
1.12       A sector review mission which visited Zaire in November 1981 2/
was the Bank Group's first attempt to develop a comprehensive view of
Zaire's energy sector.  Subsequently, at the invitation of the Government
and in close liaison with related institutions, a Bank-UNDP Energy Sector
Assessment mission visited Zaire in November 1984. The report of the 1984
mission was discussed with the Government in March 1986 and its findings
are incorporated in this document.
1.13      The assessment dealt with overall energy institutional issues and
analysed in depth the three crucial subsectors of power, petroleum and
biomass energy.   Principal issues which characterize the power subsector
stem from the existence of questionable past investments and from
institutional  weaknesses  and  include:   xi)  surplus  generation  and
transmission capacity of the Inga I1 power station and Inga-Shaba line
(paras. 2.06, 2.22, 2.23 and 2.24); (ii) inefficiency of existing small
thermal and hydropower stations and lack of a strategy for rehabilitation
and expansion of existing generation and transmission and distribution
facilities (paras. 2.05, 2.09, 4.05); (iii) institutional strengthening of
SNEL particularly in the areas of investment planning and finance (paras.
3.13, 4.05, 5.06 to 5.09); and, (iv) lack of an analytica'lly-based set of
policies and priorities for meeting urban household energy needs (para.
4.05).   IDA's recommendations and actions taken are described in the
2/   Zaire:  Introductory Energy Sector Review. World Bank, December 1981
(green cover).



- 5 -
referenced paragraphs.  The petroleum subsector issues included:  (i) the
shortcomings of the institutional framework; (ii) petroleum exploration and
production; (iii) importation, distribution and marketing of petroleum
products; and,  (iv) the role of the local refinery.   (paras. 1.07 and
1.20).    The  household  energy  subsector  issues  are:    {i)  regional
deforestation; (ii) fuelwood supply in Kinshasa; and, (iii) cooking stove
efficiency (paras. 1.22, 1.23 and 1.24).
1.14          Many of the measures proposed to address the above power
subsector issues are included in the proposed project which was appraised
in tandem with the energy sector assessment, and supports a strategy of
assisting SNEL to ensure reliability of supply and to achieve a balanced
regional and national development of hydropower potential.   The project
proposes to rehabilitate existing power facilities in order to prevent
failure of supply and to make optimal use of the electric energy of the
Zaire (Kivu)-Rwanda-Burundi   and   Inga-Bas   Zaire-Shaba   interconnected
systems.   Its broader power subsector objectives-strengthening SNEL by
continuing  general  management  assistance  and  through  a  long-term
development plan, cost-reflective tariffs, and training--support the Bank's
traditional concerns in the power subsector, primarily better planning and
stronger financial performance. Many of the recommendations on the other
subsectors,  primarily  petroleum  (para.  1.20)  and  household  energy
(para. 1.22) are being implemented by the Government and will be supported
under future energy sector operations.
Sector Objectives and Strategy
1.15      Strategy.  The fragmentation of responsibility among numerous and
diverse agents and the absence of an effective coordinating entity have
hindered  the  formulation  of  a  cohesive  sector  strategy.    Broad  and
ambitious objectives have been stated (integration of areas of low
electricity availability and doubling of connections in Kinshasa area from
60,000 in 1984 to 120,000 by 1987, expansion of petroleum exploration,
rationalization of forestry management) without the well-conceived policies
and incentives to fully achieve them. Progress is nevertheless being made
with the adoption of a liberalized pricing policy to end the uniform
national  prices  of  petroleum  products  and  electricity  which  have
discouraged adequate supply to outlying areas.   Petroleum product prices
are now based on reference prices (similar to landed costs) plus a
distribution cost differential.   Electricitv price structure adjustments
are  gradually  decreasing   the  cross-subsidization  between  consumer
categories which benefit low voltage users, and experimentation is being
tried with differentiated tariffs for diesel and hydro-supplied power.
Quarterly adjustments for petroleum products and semi-annual reviews of
electricity  rates will keep prices in line with actual costs.   Much,
however, remains to be done to ensure reliable low cost supplies of energy
products and allocate them effectively among consumers.  To this end, the
Government recognizes that top priority should be given to strategy and
policy recommendation functions of the NEC (para. 1.05) and is following up
on the recommendations of the energy sector assessment to initiate this
work. Bank Group assistance to develop a sector wide strategy and improve
planning has been requested and is being considered for inclusion in an
energy project under preparation.



1.16  Bank Group Objectives.  The Bank's objectives can be summarized as
follows:
(i) developing the NEC as an effective agent for intra-sector
coordination, strategy development, policy formulation and
planning;
(ii) encouraging adoption of comprehensive policies for energy
pricing, leading progressively to full price-liberalization;
rational production and exploitation for energy of wood
resources; for better defining the respective roles of the
private sector and Government in energy management and
development;
(iii) preparing and agreeing subsector and sector wide investment
programs based on practicable least-cost production and
supply plans;
(iv) improving the reliability of supply, efficiency, and export
earnings of the power subsector through optimal utilization
of existing assets and rehabilitation of infrastructure,
including utilization of the surplus capacity of Inga II for
increased household, industrial and export uses;
Cv) strengthening SNEL in the areas of finance, procurement and
manpower development;
(vi) formulating a least-cost petroleum supply strategy including
the roles of the domestic refinery, transport infrastructure
and the distribution companies; and,
(vii) developing a rational framework for increasing private sector
activity in the exploitation, supply and marketing of
petroleum and wood energy resources.
These objectives are consistent with the Bank's overall strategy for Zaire
and the conclusions of the energy sector assessment (para. 1.13) and are
incorporated in the proposed project where applicable.
Energy Resources
1.17      Zaire's potential energy resources are substantial (Annex 1.2).
Among its largest indigenous energy sources is its substantial hydroelec-
tric potential of 774,000 GWh/yr.   This represents almost half of the
hydroelectric potential for the entire African continent, and about 12Z of
estimated annual hydroelectric production worldwide.   The country also
possesses oil and coal resources which are unexplored except for oil
resoucces at the coast and some coal mines in the Shaba region.  Although
the total national reserves of crude oil are not well established,
commercially recoverable crudes are estimated to amount to about 74 million
barrels. Commercially mineable beds of coal, situated principally in the
Shaba region, are estimated at 720 million tons. The country contains over
50% of the forest resources of Sub-Saharan Africa.  The mixed tropical
hardwood rain forests in the Central Basin (100 million ha) and peripheral



mixed species forests (22 million ha) form the bulk of remaining forest
reserves; however, the former Mayumbe forests (240,000 ha) in the Bas Zaire
region are close to depletion due to excessive felling. Only about half of
the remaining forests are currently accessible for economic exploitation.
Other potential, as yet unexploited, energy sources include shale deposits,
tar sands, the natural gas of Lake Kivu, and solar energy. Zaire also owns
uranium resources which have been exported  for many years.   Finally,
promising sources of geothermal energy and non-wood biomass energy have
been identified, although their full potential and the extent of actual and
potential utilization (in the case of biomass) have yet to be defined.
Energy Demand
1.18       Zaire is a net exporter of electric power (para. 2.11) and an
importer of coal and coke for the copper smelters in the Shaba region. As
shown in Table 1.1 (para. 1.03), in petroleum volume terms Zaire is a net
petroleum exporter, but in value terms the net surplus is insignificant
(para.  1.20).   Zaire currently imports all of its finished petroleum
product requirements since its own crude cannot be processed economically
in the local refinery (para. 1.07). Because of the high cost of refined
imports which increased from 3% of total imports in 1972 to 232 in 1982
(representing about 232 of total merchandise imports), and an inefficient
distribution system, total demand has not been met.
1.19       Firewood, charcoal, crop and animal residues account for about
86% of Zaire's total energy consumption.  Firewood is the major fuel for
rural populations and urban peripheral households, and charcoal for the
urban centers.   A large proportion of urban households with access to
electricity or kerosene prefer woodfuels for cooking at present relative
prices, for both cost and traditional reasons. The pressure of increasing
wood-fuel demand from urban populations is shown by the distance of sources
of supply to Kinshasa (up to 150 km), Kolwezi and Lubumbashi (a radius of
depletion of up to 100 km), and to high density populations in Kivu. About
35 km from Kinshasa, charcoal prices (in 45 kg sacks) are Z3 to Z4 per kg
(about USS60-80 per ton) while in the Kinshasa market they range from Z14
to Z15 per kg (about US$280-300 per ton).  The issues in this vital area
are discussed in paras. 1.22-1.24.
Energy Supply and Related Issues 3/
1.20      Petroleum Supply. In 1983 Zaire imported 44,700 toe of crude oil
and 685,000 toe of refined products and exported over 1.2 million toe of
crude.   Internal consumption is accounted for 63% by transport, i3% by
mining, 11% by other industry and about 7% each by households and
agriculture. Since the late 1970s, petroleum product consumption remained
nearly constant at a level of about 700,000 tons per annum with foreign
exchange scarcity as the main constraint to supply.  Nearly all regions
outside Bas Zaire, the entry point for most product, experienced shortages
because of: (i) uniform national pricing which created no incentive to
transport beyond the Kinshasa market; and (ii) the physical constraints of
the distribution network. To reduce costs and improve supply within Zaire,
3/ Power subsector supply and related issues are detailed in Chapter II.



-8-
measures were introduced in June 1985 which eliminated the import monopoly
of the national oil company, PetroZaire, the fixed market quotas of the
five marketing companies and the uniform national prices for products.
Additional savings could be achieved through market-oriented reforms which
are still needed to encourage entry of new suppliers, price competition
among marketing companies and competitive bidding for product imports.
Product movement is also costly and unreliable because of inefficient
lightering by barges, the deteriorated state of the Matadi-Kinshasa
pipeline, lack of adequate storage, and logistical problems along the
distribution route, particularly on the Voie Nationale on Zaire River.
Some pipeline rehabilitation and storage additions are being financed with
private capital of the marketing companies, and improved product unloading
and other storage needs are also being reviewed.
1.21      Exploration activities have been carried out in the areas of the
Coastal and Central Basins and Tanganyika Graben. The Coastal Basin is the
only area under production, with operations offshore and onshore.
Exploration of the Central Basin, dating from the 1950s, has been
inconclusive, but recent revival of aeromagnetic and gravimetric surveys
indicates renewed oil company interest; this will be pursued further
through  IDA-financed  data  reinterpretations.       Similar  IDA-supported
activities have had positive results for the Zaire segment of the Northern
Tanganyika Graben for which companies have submitted acreage offers.
1.22      Household Energy.   Household consumption accounts for about 80%
of the final demand for energy comprised almost exclusively of wood
products originating from natural forests. Urban areas are associated with
a highly concentrated demand, primarily for charcoal, which results in a
drawdown of wood resources at rates exceeding natural regeneration in the
readily accessible hinterland.   Rural areas have been less affected thus
far because of the dispersed population, relative abundance of woody
biomass, and the traditional practice of gathering wood without felling
trees, but threats to future rural supply are nonetheless present from
destructive agricultural clearing and over-exploitation of forests by
industries. Although in a global context, Zaire's deforestation is not at
the critical level of other African countries, the trend for natural
forests near  to urban areas  is one of accelerated degradation.   The
Government   is  presently  without  adequate  strategy,   policies   and
institutions  for  coping  with  the  issues  of  forestry  production  and
exploitation.    The Government's main attempts to date to address  the
energy-related problems caused by deforestation have been in the Kinshasa
region for which it has plans to accelerate electrificatior: (para. 2.23)
and thus reduce the pressure on traditional fuelwood resources.  This is
optimistically expected to have a major impact on the demand for charcoal
only in the medium term (ten years). A complementary (or alternative) plan
to supply Kinshasa with wood energy through development of fuelwood
plantations on the Bateke Plateau, 150 km from Kinshasa is under
consideration, and the Government has requested Bank Group assistance for
this operation which will include institutional and policy assistance.
1.23       Carefully controlled exploitation of existing forest reserves in
the Cuvette Centrale and new development of plantations on the Bateke
Plateau would address Kinshasa's short and longer term wood energy needs.
Small, monitored projects in the Cuvette Centrale should be carefully



- 9 -
evaluated with a view to supply Kinshasa's charcoal needs in the medium to
long-term.     Such projects could exploit present logging residues  in
existing concessions and harvest some of the non-commercial timber as
well.   The Government,  through the supervisory role for the National
Reforestation Service (NRS), should evaluate the participation of small
entrepreneurs and private industry if such projects are feasible by
granting permits for cutting and re-planting operations, and for on-site
charcoal production. Comparative economic costs would be further defined
and pilot projects proposed along with a household energy planning program
under the proposed project (para. 4.05).
1.24       Supply increases and resource conservation over the next five
years could come from more efficient charcoal production and the wider and
more efficient use of logging and wood processing residues as fuels.
Demand side options to moderate cooking fuel consumption could include
expanded use of electricity and adoption of improved woodfuel, electric and
kerosene stoves.  Lack of reliable data, both on forest and other biomass
fuel resources, and on the level and pattern of household energy demand,
constrain  policy  development,   planning  and  programming.  Technical
assistance is urgently needed for woodfuel resource assessment and project
analysis, household energy demand surveys, strategy planning, investment
programming and policy formulation.   Assistance for a household energy
study will be initiated under the proposed project and followed up under
future energy sector operations which will emphasize planning, programming
and policy formulation.
II. THE POWER SUBSECTOR
General
2.01      The power subsector is the responsibility of the DEpartement des
mines  et  de  l'energie  (DME)  (para.  1.04).    The  SociStg  Nationale
d'Electricitd (SNEL), wholly Government owned, is responsible for the
generation, transmission and distribution of electricity throughout Zaire.
A complete description of the organization may be found in Chapter III.
Furthermore, Zaire is a member of Energie des pays des grands lacs (EGL)
which coordinates energy matters on a regional basis with the Governments
of Burundi and Rwanda and is a shareholder with these Governments in the
Societd internationale d'Alectricitd des pays des grands lacs (SINELAC)
which is implementing the Ruzizi II Regional Hydroelectric Project (Annex
2.1).
Access of Service
2.02        Electric power supply is concentrated primarily in two areas:
Kinshasa and the surrounding area of Bas Zaire in the west and the Shaba
region in the south-eastern part of the country.   About 95% of the all
electrical energy is sold in the aforementioned regions. The Shaba region
alone accounts for about 73% of SNEL's total sales, with 57% attributable
to one consumer, GECAMINES, which mines copper and cobalt and whose exports
account for 64% of Zaire's total foreign exchange earnings. In the regions
of Kasai Oriental, Kasai Occidental, Equateur, Haut Zaire and Bandundu,



- 10 -
electricity is available only in small isolated areas from local hydro and
diesel installations. The Kivu region in the eastern part of the country,
is part of an interconnected regional network which serves Kivu (Zaire),
Rwanda and Burundi.
2.03      In 1985 only about 3% of the estimated 30.7 million inhabitants
in Zaire had access to electricit; 4/ this percentage varies from one
region to another, but is very low even in major cities.   The highest,__
percentage is 13% in Kinsbasa, and except for Shaba, Kinshasa and Bas
Zaire, less than 1% of the remaining 19 million inhabitants have access to
electricity. The situation is even worse in the Kivu region, because the
isolated Kivu transmission and distribution network is very limited.
Zaire's average estimated per capita consumption of electrical energy was
144 kWh in 1985, however, consumption in the Kivu region was only 8 kWh per
capita owing to limited distribution and industrial activity in the region.
Existing Generation Facilities
2.04      The total installed capacity in Zaire is 2,599 MW of which 2,482
KW (95Z) is hydro and the remaining 117 MW (5%) is thermal, mainly diesel.
In 1985 supply from the thermal plants was only 0.4% of the total output
(Table 2.1).   Twenty-seven hydro power plants with a total installed
capacity of 80 MW and many thermal power plants of a total installed
capacity of about 59 MW are owned and operated by private companies for
their own electricity needs.   SNEL has a total installed generating
capacity of 2,460 MW of which 2,402 MW (98Z) is hydro and the remainder
diesel (58 MW). A detailed list of all the generating facilities in the
country, may be found in Annex 2.2, and those owned by SNEL are shown on
IBRD Map No. 18989.  Annual generating capability of the SNEL plants is
about 11,300 GWh.  However, in 1985, the total demand was only 1,050 MW and
total generation was 4,800 GWh (Annex 2.3), pointing to a large excess
installed generation capacity (para. 2.06).

2.05       The largest hydroelectric plants are concentrated in the Bas
Zaire/Kinshasa and Shaba regions. The Bas Zaire/Kinshasa region has the
largest installed capacity including Zongo (75 MW), Inga 1 (350 MW), and
Inga II (1,424 MW) which were commissioned in 1965, 1974 and 1982
respectively.   The four hydropower plants of the Shaba region (Nseke: 248
MW; Nzilo: 108 MW; Mwandigusha: 68 MW; and Koni: 42 MU) are being
rehabilitated under the IDA Shaba Power System Rehabilitation Project (CR
1224-ZR; para. 1.10). The Ruzizi I hydroplant in Kivu (28.2 MW) would be
rehabilitated within the proposed project (para. 4.05). SNEL also owns and
operates 5 hydro (totaling 58 MW) and 32 diesel (totaling 58 MW) power
stations which supply the rest of the cities, including some very large
ones, such as Kananga, Mbuji-Mayi, Mbandaka and Kisangani. Most of the
isolated plants date from the 1960's and perform poorly due to lack of
maintenance and spares. Nearly all SNEL's power plants, including Inga I,
Inga II and Ruzizi I, but pn-ticularly the isolated thermal and hydro
plants are in need of rehabilitation owing to limited technical capability
and irregular maintenance during periods of foreign exchange scarcity.
SNEL's present practice has been to meet maintenance needs on an ad hoc
basis without any plan or priorities.         A systematic review of the
rehabilitation needs is being ca-ried out within the power development plan
(para. 3.13).
2.06      In 1974, the Government decided to construct the 1,424 MW Inga II
hydroelectric station, together with the Inga-Shaba transmission system
(SIS) comprising a 1,700 km, 1,000 kV direct current (D.C.) line running
from the Inga II power station in western Zaire to Kolvezi (Shaba) in the
south-eastern part of the country, with converter stations at Inga and
Kolwezi.   Related investments also included reinforcements of the Shaba
region high voltage system with a new 220 kV transmission line and
installation of up-to-date teleconmrmications and control system. Eight
units of 178 NW each and the SIS were commissioned in 1982-1983. The total
cost of this program was about US$2 billion in 1983 prices,5/ primarily
financed by a consortium of banks led by the U.S. EXIM Bank and the
Governments of Italy and Sweden.   However, the load forecast used to
justify the project was based on overly optimistic expectations of the
expansion of the copper mines and smelters, rapid expansion of the economy
and new industrial development in an export free zone (ZOFI; para. 2.22).
Today Inga II generates only about 190-200 MW (about 142 of its capacity)
to supply the D.C. transmission line to Shaba.   The surplus installed
5/ The Inga II dam and power station, US$790 million; SIS, US$1,200
million; both in 1983 prices.



-12-
capacity at the Inga II hydropower station is thus about 1,225 MW with an
annual generation capability of about 7,500 GWh. Its underutilization is a
cause of concern, not only because it involves inefficient use of expensive
capital, but also because destructive cavitation phenomena take place in
turbines operating at low load.   The Government wishes to increase the
utilization of the existing capacity by increasing the demand for power.
Among the alternatives under consideration are:  (i) the establishment of
industries under the provisions of the Zone Franche d'Inga (ZOFI;
para. 2.22); (ii) accelerated expansion of the distribution network in
Kinshasa (para. 2.23); (iii) substitution of gas oil and fuel oil-fired
boilers and furnaces by electrical boilers and furnaces in industry,
particularly in the Kinshasa area (para. 2.25); (iv) power exports to Congo
(para. 2.26); and, (v) increased utilization of the SIS (tapping at Kananga
and exports to Zimbabwe, paras. 2.27 and 2.28).   A variety of proposed
prices are associated with these alternatives and the viability of the
schemes depend to a large extent upon the cost of Inga's surplus power
(para.  2.22).    Further  details  on  the  schemes  are  given  in  the
referenced paragraphs.
Transmission
2.07      The total length of SNEL's transmission lines at the end of 1984
was  4,971  km.   As  SNEL  was  formed  from  several  private  companies
(para. 3.02), voltage levels vary among the various lines (Annex 2.4).
This causes only minor problems because of non-uniformity of spare parts
for the transformers. The transmission system has received sporadic
maintenance but requires only minor rehabilitation.
2.08       More than 34% of the transmission network is comprised of the
Inga-Shaba direct current transmission line (SIS) which connects Bas Zaire/
Kinshasa to Shaba (para. 2.06). The line is capable of carrying the full
design load of 1,120 MW. Present converter station capacity is limited to
560 MW; but, when required, additional units could be installed in two
stages:  840/1120 MW.   Presently the line transmits about 200 MW and
150 GWh/month, or about 24% of its design capacity as two generating units
in the Shaba power plants have been shut down for rehabilitation
(para. 1.10).  The reliability and thus the availability of the SIS has
steadily increased over the months since it was put into service in 1982.
Maintenance and operation of the SIS is carried out by a private company at
a cost of about US$1.2 million per month.   At its current level of
transmission, the cost of transporting energy from Inga to Shaba via the
SIS is about USt0.8/kWh, excluding the capital cost of the line itself. As
GECAMINES is the major consumer served by the SIS, an increase in copper
production would result in greater utilization of the transmission line;
however, this is unlikely to happen over the next five years or as long as
the international copper market remains depressed. Rehabilitation of the
existing hydrostations in the Shaba region, requiring a shutdown of at
least 140-230 MW over a three to four year period, necessitates making up
the shortfall of power supplies through the SIS through 1985-1989. At the
end of this period, the Shaba region would require only nominal amounts of
power from the SIS, making continued use financially unattractive. It is



-13-
proposed that alternative uses for the line e.g. tapping in Kananga (para.
2.27) and exports to neighbouring countries (para. 2.28), be reviewed to
determine the long-term options of the SIS. With financing from the funds
of CR 1224-ZR, SNEL has set up a panel of experts to study the optimal
operations of the main interconnected system (Inga-Bas Zaire-Kinshasa a.c.
system/Inga-Shaba d.c. line/Shaba a.c. system), including immediate actions
to correct maintenance and operating problems which have caused supply
threatening deterioration in the Inga I and Inga II facilities.   The
recommendations of the first phase of the study (Annex 7.1); by the panel
were discussed during negotiations and funds for urgent conservation/
rehabilitation works, necessary materials and spares, and follow up studies
have been included in the proposed project (para. 4.03).
Distribution
2.09      Although forty-eight towns had distribution networks by end-1985,
the bulk of the distribution consumers is concentrated in Kinshasa and
Lubumbashi  (Annex 2.5).   Practically all transmission and distribution
systems and particularly those associated with isolated thermal and
hydroplants need rehabilitation owing to lack of maintenance and technical
expertise, resulting in high losses and unreliable supply. Although some
work is being undertaken (para. 2.13), this has been carried out without
extensive planning and prioritization, and further rehabilitation is
required.   A systematic review of rehabilitation needs of the transmission
and distribution systems would be carried out within the preparation of the
long-term power development plan (para. 3.13). The distribution networks
in the Kivu region require extensive rehabilitation and need to be extended
to meet the present unserved demand and expected growth in demand
(para. 2.16).
Connections and Sales
2.10       At the end of 1985 the total number of consumers was 116,509.
Out of this total, connections to consumers supplied from high, medium, and
low voltages were 9, 1,194 and 115,306, respectively. The sales to various
categories of consumers are summarized in Table 2.2.  Sales to GECAMINES
were 2,520 GWh, representing 57% of SNEL's total sales in Zaire.  Eighty
percent of the medium voltage consumers are located in the Bas
Zaire-Kinshasa and Shaba regions consuming 93% of the power at this voltage
level.   The average annual consumptions per consumer for the MV and LV
consumers are 0.8 GWh and 7,345 kWh respectively. In view of the very low
level of access to electricity the high average consumption of LV consumers
shows that they consist mainly of commercial enterprises and households
with relatively high incomes.  The total ntumber of consumers in the Kivu
region was 8,840 in 1985.  Out of this, SO consumers were supplied from
medium voltage.   Sales in Kivu amounted to 40.4 GWh.  Annual average
consumption for low voltage consumers in the Kivu region was about
2,450 kWh, showing again that mainly commercial enterprises and households
with relatively high incomes have access to electricity.   Summary data are
given in Annex 2.6.

Exports and Imports of Electrical Energy
2.11      SNEL has exported small amounts of power to neighboring countries
(Congo, Angola, Burundi and Rwanda) for several years, but this has only
amounted to 2-3Z of the total sales and fluctuated over the years (Annex
2.6). In addition, since 1976, there have been exchanges with Zambia based
on equalized exports and imports.   Prospects  for future exports are
summarized in para. 2.15.
Losses
2.12      Losses have varied between 5Z and 12Z for SNEL and 8% and 38% for
the Kivu region.  The variations reflect the presence of unbilled and
unmetered connections, and the fragility of the readings and data base.
The rehabilitation and expansion of the distribution networks will
alleviate the technical losses.   For the administrative losses caused by
underbilliug,  it  was  agreed  during  negotiations  that  the  consumer
inventory,'collection improvement program presently being carried out in
Kinshasa an! Lubumbashi (para. 3.16) will be expanded to the Kivu region
with a target completion date of December 31, 1986.
Works in Progre!ts
2.13           EVEL's  construction  in  progress  consists  primarily  of
rehabilitation of its aging hydroTower and transport systems (Inga I, Shaba
network, self-financed repair End replacement to small stations) and
transmission/distribution extensi.n (reinforcement of Kinshasa network, Bas
Zaire electrification, Bukavu-Katana line). In addition, SNEL is in the
early stages of a large and ambitious distribution extension project for
the accelerated electrification of Kinshasa (para. 2.23) and construction
of medium voltage lines and substations to supply electrical boilers and
furnaces; the objective of th4 latter project is to substitute hydro based
electricity for gas oil or fuel oil (para. 2.25).  Works for about 40,000



-15-
connections and the construction of medium voltage lines to supply the
electrical boilers to be installed in many industries are being financed by
loans obtained from the African Development Bank and the Italian
Government.   Although SNEL has recently started  the preparation of a
long-term power development plan to the year 2000 with assistance financed
under CR 1224-ZR (para. 3.13), all the works presently in progress have
been initiated without such a plan. Provision will be made under the
proposed project to continue work on the long term plan and for its review
by IDA.
Historical Demand for Electrical Power
2.14      The total demand for electrical energy in Zaire followed general
economic trends and, in particular, those of the copper industry. Demand
stagnated between 1974 to 1977, and decreased substantially in 1978
(-5.6%), which were the peak years of Zaire's economic crisis; then
increased between 1979 to 1981 as economic recovery gradually set in,and
reached 3,931 GWh in 1981. Details are given in Annex 2.6.

In 1982, total demand decreased once again as the economy stagnated.
However, it recovered in 1983, and in 1984 the increase over the previous
year was 11.3%.   Total sales in Zaire, reached 4,426 GWh in 1985, an
all-time maximum (Table 2.3).   These last increases correspond to the
economic growth under the stabilization program, launched by the Government
in 1983, to GECAMINES' increased productions with respect to that in 1983,

and also to the progress made under the consumer inventory, billing and
collection improvement program (para. 3.16). The average annual increases
were 4.5% and 4.1% for the 1974-1985 and 1979-1985 periods, respectively.
The total demand in the Goma-Bukavu-Uvira-Kiliba area increased by about
13X per year between 1979 and 1985 from 19.7 GWh to 40.4 GWh owing mainly
to the entry into operation in December 1981 of an clectrical boiler at the
Bralima brewery in Bukavu. Excluding the boiler, the demand in the area
increased at an average rate of 10% per year in the 1979-1984 period,
reflecting Kivu's relatively high potential for growth.
Load Forecast
2.15      SNEL.  Load forecasts available to SNEL at the time of appraisal
were neither homogeneous nor reliable having been carried out for the
various systems and regions, by various consultants, in different periods,
covering varying time periods. Therefore, the preparation of new forecasts
is a priority of the long-term development plan for the power subsector
which   is   being   prepared,   with   assistance   from   consultants
(Tractionel-Belgium),  funded  under  Credit  1224-ZR  (paras.  1.10  and
3.13). The appraisal mission reviewed the available forecasts and SNEL's
revised investment program.  The most important assumption for the three
scenarios ('high', 'medium', and 'low') considered was the commissioning of
ZOFI industries in 1990, 1993 or later, which substantially affected the
supply-demand picture. However, a move toward a more general industrial
promotion role for ZOFI (para. 2.22) will lower considerably forecast
industrial consumption. In this case, the 'low' scenario which is based on
extrapolation of the recent growth in demand into the next decade, seems to
be the most likely.   Details for the 'low' growth scenario are given in
Annex 2.7 and summarized in Table 2.4 on the following page.  Exports to
Congo are projected to be around 90 GWh/year until 1991 (para. 2.26), then
will decrease to about 25 GWh/year as Congo's own installed capacity
increases. Exports to Burundi and Rwanda are projected to decrease in the
1988-1991 period (para. 2.17) when Rwegura and Ruzizi II hydroplants will
be commissioned;  later  these  exports will increase again.   Marginal
increases are expected in the sales to the high voltage consumers (0.5% per
annum over the 1985/2000 year period), with limited demand growth from
GECAMINES. Medium voltage sales are forecast to increase by about 4.4% per
annum during the same period while low voltage sales are projected to
increase on average by about 7.3% per annum.  Overall, the average annual
increase of the total sales in Zaire is estimated at 2.7% p.a. in 1986-1988
period reaching 3.0% p.a. in 1991. The shares of sales from the HV, MV and
LV are expected to change to 41%, 21% and 33% in the year 2000,
respectively.  By that time exports will make up about 2Z of the total
sales.   Fending further analysis as part of the Power Development Plan
(para. 3.13), IDA considers this to be the most likely scenario.

carry out a detailed study of the least cost development of the power
system in the Kivu region, in the light of construction of the Ruzizi II
Regional Hydropower Project, including a detailed review of the load
forecast for the Kivu area in Zaire. The consultants forecast that demand
in the area, including presently unserved demand, would increase from 46
GWh in 1985 to 93 GWh in 1995 at 6.7% per year in the 1985-1990 period and
thereafter at 7.5% per year to 1995.  The appraisal mission reviewed this
forecast and in the light of latest information about the possibility of a
pharmaceutical company installing an electric boiler (6 MW) at its plant in
Bukavu and recommissioning of the cement plant in Katana, introduced
brought some slight modifications with  respect  to  gradual  increments
created by these two projects. It is forecast that demand will increase by
8% per year in the 1986-1990 per4od and by 9% per year between 1991 and
1995, resulting in an average annual growth of 8% for the 1986-1995
period. It is also expected that the consumer inventory/billing/collection
improvement program being implemented in 1986 in the Kivu region
(para. 2.12) will alleviate the administrative losses and create a
relatively high increase in 1986. Details are given in Annex 2.9 and the
forecast is summarized in Table 2.5.
