Tariffs and Long-Run Marginal Cost
6.05        While future electricity pricing is projected to meet SNEL's
financial requirements (para. 5.08), all past financial and economic costs
have not been covered owing to large uneconomic investments in generation
(Inga II) and transmission (Inga-Shaba line) which exceed the capacity of
the consumer to finance and therefore have been and will continue to be
subsidized by the Government.  The present tariff structure includes an
element of cross-subsidization of low voltage residential   consumers who
pay less than medium voltage consumers in spite of higher cost/kWh for low
voltage power, particularly when high supply cost regions (supplied mainly
by small thermal and hydro plants) are subsidized by low supply cost
regions through a uniform national tariff policy for low voltage
residential rates.   Medium voltage industrial users also benefit from
theregion-l subsidization of a uniform national tariff but only during
critical hours of consumption (Annex 5.5).  SNirL's move to marginal cost
pricing is likely to bring significant increases in the cost of low voltage
power, which for social reasons could not be immediately and fully
reflected in the tariff to household consumers; some differential in
contribution above the cost of service by the other user categories may
therefore  be  necessary.    Maintaining  uniform  national  tariffs  has
discouraged investment as well as efficient operation in high cost regions
which are perceived   by SNEL as a burden on its finances; virtually no



-46-
maintenance has been carried out on isolated diesel plants which are very
often operated at low capacity as a cost saving measure.  Negotiable MV
rates for operation exceeding critical hours are being tried to determine
the  effectiveness  of  differentiated  cost-based  tariff.    Should  this
experiment prove successful, SNEL will consider complete regional
differentiation in tariffs for allindustrial and commercial customers, and
as far as practicable for household consumers with consumption rates in
excess of a fixed minimum.
6.06         Consultants (EdF International) have undertaken a study of
marginal cost pricing for power based on estimated load forecasts and a
preliminary investment program prepared by SNEL and peak/off-peak
variations.  However, SNEL's investment program appears ambitious in light
of capital constraints and its load forecasts optimistic in view of past
experience, and IDA has proposed a downward revision (para. 3.13). based
on the reduced investment program and load growth, the LRMC of power is
estimated to be about US.5.0.   It is assumed  that SNEL would adopt
progressive tariff increases such that its average tariff would approach
the LRMC by the mid-1990's (para. 5.11). Funds would be provided under the
proposed project to update the LRMC study once the long-term development
plan is completed in mid-1986 (Annex 4.1).
Rehabilitation of the Shaba Power Plants and Transmission System
6.G7         Return on Investment.   The economic rate of return is the
discount rate at which the present economic value of the project and
operating costs equals the present value of savings and revenues expected
to be obtained from the project.   rhe ERR for the Shaba Rehabilitation
Project was determined as 16% in the Staff Appraisal Report No. 3400a-ZR
dated March 15, 1982. As costs and tariffs have changed since then, the
ERR was recomputed.  The benefits are computed as the revenues from the
sale of electricity which is available due to the rehabilitation program.
The ERR is estimated to be about 12% and it is acceptable. The reason for
the decrease in the ERR is that project costs increased and completion and
therefore realization of benefits of the project are delayed.  Armex 6.5
contains details on the foregoing computations and assumptions.
Project Risks
6.08       The physical risk associated with the rehabilitation and exten-
sion components of this project and risks of higher costs due to receiving
bids after presentation to the Board are small because detailed inspections
were carried out for the power plants and the work to be completed for the
distribution  networks  is  routine  and  only  proven  elements  would  be
included.    The main risk would be institutional in nature, as SNEL is
generally weak in administration and project management.   However, this
risk would be minimized by provision of compete.it consultants to assist
SNEL in carrying out the project and SNEL would continue to receive
management  assistance  to improve  its performance  (Annex  4.1).        An
additional risk relates to the Government's willingness to adopt rational
investment planning and pricing policies for the sector.  Government has



-47-
demonstrated a commitment to improving its performance in these areas by
seeking advice through the Energy Assessment and engaging in a dialogue on
pricing  and  financial  policy  reforms,  preparation  of  a  long-term
development plan, and comnitting itself to exchanging views with IDA in the
future (para. 1.13).
VII. AGREEMENTS REACHED
7.01      During negotiations, agreement was reached with the Borrower
chat:
(a) it would conclude with SNEL by '.ne 30, 1987 an agreement to (i)
specify ownership of all assets financed by the Government, but
which are operated by SNEL; (ii) establish responsibility for
payment of existing debt service until all outstanding loans are
fully repaid; (iii) define the broad objectives of SNEL's tariff
policy and (iv) transfer the Inga II hydroplant and Inga Shaba
line to SNEL at a transfer price based on their value in use
(para. 3.15);
(b) all grants, credits and loans obtained by the Government for the
purpose of financing SNEL's 1986-92 investment program would not,
without IDA's consent, be onlent to SNEL at a rate of interest of
less than 5 percent (para. 5.10); and,
Cc) it would exchange views with IDA on any investment in
the power sector greater than US$5 million, including its
financial and economic justification, proposed financing plan,
sources of financing and amount of debt service (para. 3.13).
7.02     During negotiations, agreement was reached with SNEL that:
(a) the customer inventory/collection improvement program would be
extended to include the Kivu region would be compieted by
December 31, 1986 (para. 2.19);
(b) the price of power supplied to ZOFI from Inga II would be
adjusted to reflect long-run marginal cost at the time Inga's
capacity is fully committed and investments in additional
facilities for the supply and delivery of electricity are
required (para. 2.22);
(c) during preparation of SNEL's long-term development plan, the
timing and financial impact of the Kinshasa elentrification
program on SNEL and the Government would be examined to determine
the optimal phasing of the program and to limit investment in
accordance with that phasing; IDA, the Government and SNEL would
exchange views and agree on the best way to proceed with the
program (para. 2.24);



-48-
(d) the long-term development plan will be completed by September 30,
1986 and furnished to the Association for an exchange of views
(para. 3.13);
(e) SNEL would exchange views annually with IDA on the power sector
development plan (para. 3.13);
(f) SNEL would, prior to undertaking any investment in the power
seL.-r greater than US$5 million, submit the investment to IDA
for approval along with an economic and financial justification
and agreement on the Government and SNEL's obligations for
financial charges (para. 3.13);
(g) beginning in 1987, SNEL would cover from its own resources all
operating and maintenance charges associated with the Inga II
hydroplant and Inga-Shaba transmission line and reimburse over a
ten year period a portion of the original debt related to
construction of the Inga II hydroplant corresponding to its value
in use (para. 3.15);
(h) SNEL would prepare by July 31, 1986 an interim action plan to
resume tiaining activities for technical personnel and managers
and following an exchange of views with IDA begin implementing
the plan by September 1, 1986 and would furnish by November 1,
1986 to IDA for exchange of views a draft long-term Master Plan
for training (para. 3.16);
,i) SNEL's accounts would be adjusted in accordance with standard
accounting practice and in a manner acceptable to IDA to reflect
the agreements reached between SNEL and the Government on the
transfer price and ftnancial charges for Inga II and the
Inga-Shaba line (para. 5.04);
Cj) SNEL would not incur debt unless internal cash generated for the
twelve months preceding incurrence of the debt is at least 1.5
times the maximum debt service requirements for any succeeding
year (para. 5.06);
(k) it would conclude with the Government an action program ro reduce
receivables such that receivables would not exceed 4 months'
sales by end-1986, 3 months' sales by end-1987 and 2.5 months'
sales by end-1988 and thereafter (para. 5.07); and,
(1) SNEL would take all measures including adjustment of the
structure and level of its tariffs required to produce for each
of its fiscal years beginning in 1986 funds from internal sources
equivalent to about 30% of the annual average of capital
expenditures incurred or to be incurred during that year and the
two next following fiscal years (para. 5.11).

7.03      As special conditions of Credit Effectiveness:
(a) a subsidiary loan agreement would be signed by the Government and
SNEL setting the terms and conditions of onlending the IDA Credit
(para. 4.09);
(b) the colender's agreement wuld be executed (para. 4.09); and,
(c) SNEL and the Government would furnish to IDA the action program
for the reduction of receivables (para. 5.07).
7.04       With the above agreements, the project is suitable for an IDA
Credit of SDR 37.3 million (US$37 million).

ANNEX 1.2

ZAIRE
SECOND POWER PROJECT
Energy Resources
1.         Zaire's major energy resources are hydropower and forests; it
possesses oil and coal resources which are mostly unexplored and only
partially exploited, as well as shale deposits, the methane gas of Lake
Kivu (shared with Rwanda), uraniurs, geothermal deposits and solar energy.
2.         Hydropower.  Hydropower *Ls Zaire's major energy resource and is
the source of 98Z of its electricity. The total hydropotential of Zaire is
estimated at 740,000 GWh, roughly half of Africa's hydropotential.
Although only 1.1% of the potential is developed (11,000 GWhla) Zaire is
feced with a large surplus of installed capacity (total generation was only
4,800 GWh in  1985).   The ri-2erutilizazion of the installed capacity
Cconcec.trated in western Zaire),when the major areas of the country are
sporadically served or not at all, is a cause of concern to the Government,
which is considering several alternatives to increase power consumption.
3.         Forest and Biomass Resources.   Wood and other biomass fuels are
the principal sources of energy for the population and are extremely
valuable resources to Zaire, which is the most heavily forested country of
Africa. Forests cover some one hundred and twenty-two million hectares,
more than one-half of Zaire, and are grouped in three main areas. These
are the Mayumbe forests in Bas-Zaire (240,000 ha); the Central Basin
equatorial forests in the Equateur, Haut-Zaire and Bandundu regions (101
million ha) and the peripheral forests including Shaba savannan, forest
strips and dense mountain forests in Kivu (21 million ha).
4.         Total primary pntential resources based on the growth rates are
evaluated at 66 million toe/year. An extremely precise and detailed forest
inventory is being carried out for the central basin by a Zario-Canadian
team with the objective of rationalizing exploitation.   The volume of
ascessible wood per species and regeneration rates are to be deduced from
the data. Natural accessible forest growth yield is currently estimated at
about 150 million cubic meters per year, i.e. 10 million toe, :n the
central basin alone (assuming conservative growth rate of 2.5 cubic meters
per hectare per year). An estimated 155,000 cu3 woodwaste annually comes
from the sawmills, and its use as an industrial heat and power source and
for producing domestic charcoal have been tested successfully.
5.           Bagasse from sugar-mills, rubber waste from the crepe-rubber
plants and palm-fruit waste from the oil mills are presently utilized as
biow8ss sources with potential for further development.



52
ANNEX 1.2
Page 2 of 3
6.        Petroleum Exploration and Production.  Oil was discovered in the
Coastal Basin in 1970 and production began in  1975.   In  1983 Zaire
extracted 9.3 million barrels of crude oil from the Coastal basin, 90% of
production from offshore fields exploited by Zaire-Gulf Oil (under an
exploration convention granted by the Government in 1969) and about 1OZ
from the on-shore field. Because of its high fuel oil content this crude
cannot be refined economically in Zaire's present facilities in Moanda
(capacity: 750,000 t/yr).  Based on the findings from recent exploratory
wells, production is expected to increase to 3.4 million barrels in the
onshore section and to 14 milion barrels in the offshore section by 1986.
Thereafter overall production may decline, unless further reserves are
identified.
7.         Limited exploration was carried out in the Congo Central Basin
prior to 1981 with little success; new surveys and reinterpret -tions are
underway. An airmag survey of Zaire's third sedimentary basin the Great
Lakes Basin, or Tanganyka Graben, was carried out in conjunction with
Kenya, Uganda and Tanzania financed by ongoing Bank Group operations.
Following a promotional meeting held in November 1984, several companies
have expressed interest pursuing exploration activities.
8.         Coal.  Coal is exploited in two areas in Zaire:  the open-pit
mine of Luena in Shaba (reserves estimated at 20 million tons) and the
underground mine of Lukuga (reserves estimated at about 700 million tons)
north of Kalemie.  The coal is low grade and has a high ash content.
Production, mainly from the Luena deposit, has been decreasing steadily
since 1980 because of lack of spare parts and is now about 99,000 tons per
year, whereas 135,000 tons of higher quality coal per year are imported by
Gecamines.  A third coal deposit, the reserves of which have not been
estimated, is said to exist at Walikale, north-west of Lake Kivu.  The
economic evaluation of extraction and transportation options would be the a
logical next step in analysing potential use of coal; a consultant's study
on these issues is being reviewed within the Bank at present.
Other Resources
9.        Methane.  Lake Kivu, which belongs to both Zaire and Rwanda, has
a unique methane gas potential in its deep waters. In 1975 both countries
agreed to create a joint company which wGuld allocate concessions and
participate in all exploration activities.   Potential markets - power
generation, fertilizers and chemicals, fuel for small vehicles and industry
- all require heavy investment and large operations for economies of scale,
but initial small-scale activity is necessary to allow monitoring of the
effects of exploration in the lake's environment.   A pilot extraction
installation (1963) at Cape Rubona has been rehabilitated to a production
level of 3,000 to 6,000 cubic meters per day.   Electrogaz  (Rwanda)
commissioned a study of the feasibility of expanding Rubona's production to
15,000 m3 per day and their report dated March 1984 recommending the
expansion is under consideration in the Bank. Electricite de France (EDF)
is carrying out prefeasib-ility studies for a plant at Kabeke to produce 20
million cubic meters of methane per year (total investment ECU 50 million).



53
ANNEX 1.2
Page 3 of 3
10.       Oil Shales and Tar Sands.  Reserves of 100 to 120 million tons of
oil shale are located in the Kisangani basin . Their crude oil content per
ton is estimated as between 60' and 100 liters, but further technical and
cost studies,  are needed.    In Bas-Zaire,  reserves  of tar  sands  are
estimated at 2.5 billion tons.  Experimental drillings have been done in
the asphalt sands of Mayumbe and a feasibility study of investment in
bitumen facilities in the SOZIR refinery has been studied.
11.        Geothermal Potential.  This has not been exhaustively assessed
but Northern Shaba and Kivu are said to have resources. A small geothermal
installation (peak capacity of 275 kW) has supplied the Bukena mine with
power and water since 1953. Large scale exploration does not appear to be
economical in the foreseeable future.
12.           Uranium.   Zaire has exported uranium from the Centre de
Recherches Nucleaires in Kinshasa since at least 1967, but the reserves,
estimated to be between 4,000 and 8,000 tons, have never been published.
Production of electricity from nuclear sources in Zaire is not under active
discussion.

ANNEX 2.1
Page 1 of 2
ZAIRE
SECOND POWER PROJECT
International O::yan. zations
Communaute 6conomique des pays des grands lacs (CEPGL)
1.        The Communaute &eonomique des pays des grands lacs (CEPGL), is an
organization which was established in September 1976 by Burundi, Rwanda and
Ze?'re to revive the former cooperation among the three countries which
existed under the Belgian administration prior to independence. CEPGL was
formed to promote free movement of persons and goods within the area, and
to cooperate politically and economically within the region as it relates
to other countries in Africa.   The entity is made up of a council of
ministers, one from each of the countries, which meets twice a year and
reports to an annual meeting of the presidents of the three countries - the
highest authority of the organization. The day-to-day activities including
the preparation and execution of programs and coordination within the
administration of the three countries is handled by a Secretariat located
in Giseny'., Rwanda. CEPGL receives assistance from USAID, France, Belgium
and FED for the preparation of feasibility studies.
2.           CEPGL has sponsored a glass bottle manufacturing factory in
Burundi, and in June 1981, the International Finance Corporation (IFC)
approved a loan of US$4.0 million and an equity investment of US$0.75
million for its construction. The factory was placed in service in 1983.
Another activity is exploring the potential for the production of methane
from the deep waters of Lake Kivu.    Several issues need to be resolved
before an investment program for gas development can be defined; for
example, the legal and institutional framework for gas extraction by Rwanda
and Zaire; the safety limitation and the establishment of the rate at which
the gas can be extracted; and the nature and the extent of the market and
the derivative products. The potential contribution of the development of
gas to this area is still in the distant future.
Energie des pays des grands lacs (EGL)
3.         EGL was set up to ensure the availability of electric power in
the region, and as a result has promoted the construction of the Ruzizi II
hydroelectric power project on the Ruzizi River downstream from the
existing Ruzizi I generating station on the border between Zaire and Rwanda
(Map IBRD 18989).   This organization, located in Bujumbura, Burundi, is
staffed with three technical directors - one each seconded from REGIDESO,
Burundi; ELECTROGAZ, Rwanda, and SNEL, Zaire; together with a small support
staff.



55
ANNEX 2.1
Page 2 of 2
Societe Internationale d'Electricitg des Pays des Grands Lacs (SINELAC) -
The Regional Implementing Agency of Ruzizi II
4.          The three Governents of Burundi, Rwanda and Zaire in February
1984 ratified their agreement to form the international company, SINELAC,
to be the regional implementing agency of the Ruzizi II project.
5.             SINELAC, with its head office in Bukavu, Zaire 1/ is an
international public company established for a 50-year renewable period,
and the three Governments of Burundi, Rwanda and Zaire contribute equally
to its capital. Although SINELAC has been formed initially for the Ruzizi
II  project,   it  could  acquire  and  manage  other  electric  power
installations. SINELAC is governed by the rules of the agreement and its
own by-laws, and for cases that are not covered by these rules and
regulations by the Investment Code of the Communaute &eonomique des pays
des grands lacs (CEPGL), and by the laws of the country of its
headquarters, Zaire.   SINELAC would operate as a commercial entity with
financial objectives defined initially in relation to the project and its
electricity tariffs would be established at a level which wucld produce a
satisfactory rate of return for the project.
6.        SINELAC is governed by a General Assembly consisting of the three
Ministers of Energy.   This General Assembly meets once a year and its
responsibilities are to appoint or dismiss the directors, the general
manager, the account controllers (commissaires aux comptes) and the
auditors, and to discuss the financial statements of each previous year.
The Board of Directors consists of six members, two from each country. A
three member management committee is in charge of SINELAC's day-to-day
operations. SINELAC's General Manager is appointed by the General Assembly
and the Board of Directors has the responsibility of appointing and
removing the other two members of the management committee.   The three
members of the committee would be from different countries.   SINELAC
consults with IDA before appointing a new general manager.
1/ SINELAC will operate from Bujumbura, Burundi until 1987, when the
construction of its headoffice will be completed.

ANNEX 2.8
Page 1 of 2
ZAIRE
SOCIETE NATIONALE D'ELECTRICITE (SNEL)
SECOND POWER PROJECT
Existing Generation, Transmission and Distribution
Facilities in and Exports from the KCivu Region
I. Existing Facilities
Generation
The Ruzizi I hydroelectric station (28.2 MW) serves the Goma, Bukavu
and Uvira areas of the Kivu region, which utilize about 11 MW of the power
generated (40Z of capacity); the remainder is exported to Rwanda and Burundi.
The scheme was built in 1956-1958 on the falls at the exit of the Ruzizi river
from Lake Kivu and consists of a concrete gravity type dam 14 m high and 180 m
wide, and a powerhouse with four turbo-generator units at the bottom of the
dam.  Two 6.3 MW units were comidissionned in 1958 and the last two units of 7.8
MW each were put into service in 1972. The power station suffers from a lack of
adequate maintenance and shortage of spare parts. Until March 1985, the town of
Goma in the northern part of the Kivu region was supplied by a diesel plant with
total installed capacity of 1.2 MW, but capable of producing only 300-500 kW and
a limited amount of power was imported from the Gisenyi hydro plant in Rwanda.
With the commissioning of the 110 kV Katanga-Goma transmission line (presently
being operated at 70 kV), in March 1985, Goma is also supplied frcm the
interconnected system.
Transmission
A 70 kV transmission line from the Ruzizi I power station presently
supplies Katana where a cement plant (45,000 t/a) built in 1958, but
stopped since the 1960s, will be rehabilitated and recommissioned in
1988-1989; a branch line also supplies Bukavu where the main consumer is a
brewery with an electric boiler of 8 MW. In March 1985, the line was extended
from Katana to Goma, financed by the Government of Yugoslavia.  Another 70 kV
transmission line, owned and operated by SNEL, crosses Rwanda to supply
Bujumbura, the capital of Burundi.  The Bujumburr 70/6.6 kV, 2x5.25 MVA and
6.6/15 kV, 1 MVA substation and a 15 kV line from Bujumbura to Uvira are
presently owned and operated by SNEL which intends to transfer the latter to the
Burundi national power company, Regie de distribution d'eau et d'electricite
(REGIDESO) of Burundi. The transmission system in the region, which dates from
the  1960s  has  received  sporadic  maintenance  but  requires  only  minor
rehabilitation.
Distribution
The total length of the 15 kV and 6.6 kV distribution line in the
towns of Bukavu, Uvira and Goma is 245 km; 0.4 kV distribution network amounts
to 232 km. The line lengths show that priority was given to the supply of large
consumers (industries, workshops) rather than individual household consumers.
The old parts of Bukavu are supplied by cables directly from the 6.6 kV busbars
of the Ruzizi I power station while the newer districts are supplied by cables
and lines of 15 kV through 6.6/15 kV, 10.4 MVA transformers. The 6.6 kV network



68
ANNEX 2.8
Page 2 of 2
is very old, improperly maintained, overloaded and in need of renewal.
Distribution in Uvira and Goma is also made by 6.6 kV cables which are very old
and limited to the central districts of the two towns. In Goma, a new network
of 15 kV is also being commissionned.
II. Exports
Past Exports
The interconnected network benefits primarily Rwanda and Burundi
through exports from Zaire, although SNEL has imported small amoents of
power from Rwanda for Goma. The general export trend has varied with the coming
on stream of new plants and hydrological fluctuations.   Exports to Rwanda
increased 3ubstaatially in 1979-1981 period when the Ntaruka hydro plant in
Rwanda (11 MW, 20 GWh/a) was insufficieut to meet the demand, but decreased
following the commissioning of the Mukungwa hydrostation (2x6MW, 48 GWh/a) in
Rwanda in January 1982. Exports to Burundi increased gradually until 1981 when
Ruzizi I was the only plant supplying Bujumbura, Burundi's capital and principal
electrified area. With the commissioning of the Mugere hydrostation in Burundi
in 1982 (8 MW, 41 GWh/a), exports to this country also decreased. However they
increased once again in 1984 to meet the relatively large demand of a new glass
factory which was commissioned in end-1983.   In 1985, because of a favorable
hydrology, Mugere was able to generate all year round and the utilization of the
glass factory was low; therefore exports to Burundi decreased once again. While
national resource development is an important part of Burundi and Rwanda's
strategy of reducing dependency on foreign sources for the supply of electricity
aimed at expanding the power sub-sectors, recently all three Governments
recognized the advantages of least-cost development on a regional basis and
agreed to jointly construct the Ruzizi II regional hydro power project.
Interconnections with Ruzizi II Project
The power generated at the Ruzizi II power plaat presently under
construction will be transmitted through new 110 kV lines.   The power to be
transmitted to Zaire, Burundi and Rwanda will arrive at the new Mururu II
substation on the Zaire-Rwanda border near Ruzizi I.  The existing Ruzizi I
70-kV system and the new Ruzizi II 110-kV system will be interconnected at this
substation through a 110/70 kV 10 MVA transformer which is being provided as
part of the Ruzizi II project.  The short interconnection line between Mururu
II-Ruzizi I will also be constructed within the Ruzizi II project; the 70 kV
terminal at Ruzizi I will be installed within the proposed project.

ZAIRE
SOCIETE NATIONALE D'ELECTRICITE (SNEL)
SECOND POWER PROJECT
General Management Assistance to SNEL
Fir-t Phase of the Assistance: Data Management Study
SNEL signed in November 1982, a contract with SEMA-METRA/ SOBEM&P
(SMISOBEMAP)  to  restructure  its  acounting  and  commercial  computer
organization and procedures. Although the work was intended to be directed
mainly to computer problems, it has tended to be rore towards
organization. During the year 1983, SNEL's computer centre was reorganized
by SOBEMAP so that there would be a one to one relationship between a
customer division and the centre,  and staff were trained.   As a new
experiment, incentives were given to key staff, with positive results,
which shows that crompetent local staff can be motivated by proper rewards.
The purchase of nei computers was approved to cope with future work load,
and it was ordered in June 1984; 9] the computers will be operational in
1985.
Also in 1983 much organizational work was accomplished in the
financial department, with regional computerization of general accounting
and consolidation at headquarters, establishment of individual accounts for
suppliers, personnel, projects, customers, and of simplified cost
accounting. Accounting procedures were written with the help of Helios and
manuals were prepared for easy future updating. More than 150 accountants,
cashiers and bookkeepers from all regions underwent training at Sanga,
SNEL's training centre, and all accountants were tested to evaluate the
strength of the regional accounting organizations.   But the main task of
1983 was the inventory of all HV/LV transforming stations in Kinshasa,
coding of Kinshasa streets, reorganization of the billing process and the
inventory of all customers in Kinshasa; a comprehensive investigation was
carried-out with the assistance of a full-time consultant.   It achieved
satisfactory results, as it was shown that about 15% of identified
customers were not billed and corrections of deficiencies would result by
end-1984 in an increase of 26% of the energy and 49% of the amounts
invoiced, either because customers were billed incorrectly, insufficiently
or not at all.
1/ For Kinrhasa headquarters a HP 3,000/42 computer with a 2 MBits central
and 1,200 MBits disk memory and for the regions two 0.25 HBits memory
microcomputers.

Second Phase of the Study: General Management Assistance
Difficulties encountered during first year implementation, mainly
because of lack of competent staff, emphasized the necessity for timely
starting up of the second phase of the organizational technical assistance
as provided in Section 2.03 of the Project Agreement for CR 1224-ZR. In
May 1984, SNEL and the Bank agreed on a second phase covering actions in
four main areas:
(i) personnel, for staff file organization, completing of job
descriptions, definition of training and orientation of staff
to positions and study of ways to motivate people by a higher
salary, premiums, and benefits of some kind;
(ii procurement and inventory control, for establishing detailed
procurement procedures and precise rules for inventory
coutrol;
(iii) financial, for completion of the asset register established by
Helios in the revaluation study, preparation of computerized
assets management and d;-finition of rules for project
accounting and transfer of work in progress to fixed assets;
and
(iv) management control, for establishing management indicators and
devising the principles of cost accounting to provide for
their   preparation,    and   definition    of   rules   and
responsibilities for signatures of internal memos and outgoing
mail,   to  correct   delays  resulting   from  the  present
disorganization.
Staff motivation is crucial in this plan of action, because there
are competent people in SNEL and elsewhere, but they cannot be interested,
attracted or kept without a framework within which they could be
stimulated. SNEL has created a Service of Organization and Methods (BOM)
under the Secretary general, to be composed of about 30 staff from S.N.EL.
and to be recruited outside or to be supported by expatriate staff, who are
responsible for the conception and the implementation of the project, and
who would be paid as project officers and thus would not besubject to
SNEL's conventional salary grid.
Evaluation of the Technical Assistance Programs Received by SNEL
A joint IDA/CCCE mission undertook in March 1986 the evaluation
2/ of the technical assistance programs received by SNEL since 1982 and
identified the major problems encountered by SNEL, prepared recc'umendations
to assist SNEL in finding solutions. The final report of the mission is
not ready yet but a detailed Aide-Memoire was left with SNEL.
2/ Covering the financial and commercial systems, supply/purchasing,
structure and organization, human resources and training.



79
ANNEX 3.4
Page 3 of 9
The conclusion of the evaluation was that many positive results had
been achieved since intensive management and organizational assistance was
introduced in 1982, but that execution of new programs and systems had
experienced setbacks because of a lack of physical and human resources.
SNEL management devoted several meetings to discussion of i mediate actions
It could take to reorient ongoing activities and to complete preparation of
human resource/training components being considered for the proposed
Energy I Project. The priority areas signalled by the mission which should
receive emphasis under the proposed project and future Energy I project
include:
i) Finance. The new accounting system adapted in 1984 is not
really operational as evidenced by the delays and anomalies which still
plague SNEL's accounts. The major bottleneck is that SNEL staff have not
mastered the system and require additional basic and system-specific
training.   The first priority is 'to make the numbers viable-.  Further
introduction of analytical accounting and finance systems should not be
considered until SNEL's accounts become dependable. The limited training
for accounting and finance staff included under the proposed Power II
Project will need to be broadened under Energy I.
ii)   Commercial.   The various client inventory and collection
programs are generally sound, but the maintenance of these systems has not
been adequately provided for.   Staff and logistical support is needed to
manage billing records, client accounts, etc. The connection department,
the key link of ongoing and future distribution programs, is technically
weak and could compromise commercial improvement efforts. Logistical
support   and   efficiency   improvements   for   connections   (possibly
subcontracting to private firms) are likely candidates for Energy I.
iii) Purchasing and Supply. Implementation of well-conceived and
designed supply and inventory control is blocked by lack of physical
facilities   (warehouse   improvement   and   construction   is   stopped).
Insufficient number of staff have been assigned to inventory goods.
Material and equipment losses are high. SNEL will take measures to finish
construction and rehabilitation; but assistance will be needed to implement
the purchasing and supply control program. Training of staff, particularly
in the area of procurement will be necessary.
iv)   Human Resources.   An excellent base for evaluating and
classifying personnel has been established as a first step to introducing
the new organizational structure. The next steps include the execution of
a recruitment strategy, normalization of professional qualifications and
the development of a Personnel Master Plan.  The activities will begin
under Power II, but are likely to need continued expertise under Energy I.
v)   Training.   The preparation and execution o; the interim
training plan (1986/87) will fully absorb the limited resources of the
training division staff and no work on a long-term plan will be possible
without the immediate strengthening of the T/A team.    As tne logical



80
ANNEX 3.4
Page 4 of 9
extension of the human resources activities is a longer term training plan,
existing T/A for human resources (SEMA/SOBEMAP) will be reinforced to
complete the Personnel Master Plan and develop from it a Training Master
Plan. The latter should be well advanced by appraisal in order for Energy
I to carry out a human resource planning and development program in line
with execution of the Plan Directeur de Developpement.
Based on the recommendations, greater emphasis will be placed on
(i) skills transfer and appointment of counterparts to progressively
decrease dependence on T/A and (ii) training (in the areas of accounting,
finance, procurement, computing).   Consideration will also be given to
providing the logistical support (micro computers, transportation) needed
to master and sustain the various systems and programs.
The  successful  implementation  and  continuation  of  management
improvements will depend to a large extent on the motivation of SNEL' s
staff. To this end, SNEL's salary and benefit structure, which is under
review, will need reform to ensure competitiveness and motivation of its
staff, as it appears that SNEL does not presently compare favorably with
other major parastatals.

Key areas of intervention, main objectives, terms of reference
and estimated amounts of san-months are given in pages - of this annex.

ZAIRE
SOCIETE NATIONAL D'ELECTRICITE (SNEL)
SECOND POWER PROJECT
Description of the Project
I. Rehabilitation and Extension Works
Ruzizi I Hydroelectric Power Plant
1.         Consultants (ICM) carried out a detailed inspection on the state of
the dam, powerhouse and equipment and concluded that the installation was
functioning but that reliability was very low owing to deficiencies from (i)
lack of maintenance and spare parts; and (ii) the degradations and modifications
which occurred over time such as sedimentation in the reservoir, fractures in
the concrete and corrosion in the penstocks. The fractures in the concrete are
not of a kind and level to jeopardize the operation of the plant; however, close
monitoring is necessary and instrumentation will be provided as part of the
project.   Works for the generating units will cover windings,  relays and
signalization, complete overhauling of the units and the construction of the
stator windings. The consultants concluded that if the rehabilitation was not
carried out urgently, most of the electromechanical equipment would need to be
replaced later with a cost far exceeding the proposed rehabilitation. The total
cost  of  this  rehabilitation  component,  including  physical  and  price
contingencies and engineering services, is estimated at US$8.5 million of which
US$7.8 million would be in foreign currency.
Transmission and Distribution network in the Kivu region
2.         Detailed inspection of the network and substations revealed that the
overhead transmission lines of 70 kV and 15 kV require only minor
rehabilitation.   In Bukavu,  the old 6.6 kV distribution network would be
completely replaced by new 15 kV cables and lines while the 15 kV network could
be rehabilitated; extensions to new districts would be made with 15 kV cables
and lines. The old 6.6/0.4 kV transformers would be rehabilitated and used in
Uvira. The industrial district would be connected to the existing 70/15 kV; 10
MVA substation at the Bralima brewery. In Goma, SNEL is commissioning a 15 kV
distribution ring which will supply the center and industrial district; urgent
rehabilitation work was carried out in this town in the second half of 1985 in
order to transfer to 15 kV a few consumers who are still supplied from the old
6.6 kV network. The 6.6 kV line from Goma to Sake would be upgraded to 15 kV.
For the supply of the Uvira and Kiliba areas, a 70 kV antenna line of about 17
km from the existing 70 kV Ruzizi I-Bujumbura line and 70/15 kV 5 MVA substation
would be built at Kiliba on Zairian territory.   All transformers and other
substation equipment would be inspected, rehabilitated and/or overhauled
according to needs.   The new 15/0.4 kV substations would be built in two
standard cabinet or pole-mounted designs.   Distribution networks in Bukavu,
Goma, Uvira and Kiliba wil' also be extended to supply the new districts. All
new 0.4 kV overhead lines would use preassembled cables with extra conductors
for public lighting.  In addition to the rehabilitation works, about 83 km of
new medium voltage lines and cables, about 150 km of new preassembled low
voltage cables will be constructed.  Lastly, a 70 kV terminal to interconnect
the existing Ruzizi I system with the Ruzizi II transmission system will be



87
ANNEX 4.1
Page 2 of 5
installed at Ruzizi I power station switchyard. It is estimated that the total
cost of this component (including contingencies and engineering services) would
be US$14.1 million of which US$12.7 million would be in foreign currency.
Shaba System Rehabilitation
3.             Supplementary material and works identified during the ongoing
rehabilitation (Credit 1224-ZR) include: (a) further repairs to the turbines,
generators, hydromechanical and electrical systems in all four generating
stations identified during the shutting down and dismounting of the units; (b)
modernization of the turbine speed regulation systems; (c) replacement of the
shaft of the unit No. 4 (65 MW) at Nseke power station; (d) repair and
replacement of the valves at Nzilo and Nseke dams; (e) additional installation
works by the general contractor; and (f) engineering services for the
supplementary works. Revised estimates of costs and revised financing plan for
the Shaba Power Rehabilitation Project are given in Table 1 of this Annex. The
total cost of this component, including physical and price contingencies and
engineering services, is estimated at US$6.2 million of which US$5.2 million
would be in foreign currency.
Emergency Repairs and Operational Improvements for the Inga i and Inga II
Hydropower Facilities
4.        The Inga I power plant commissioned in 1972 provides partial supply to
Kinshasa  and  exports  power  to  the Congo.    Preventive maintenance  of  the
facilities has been minimal because of lack of foreign exchange for spares and
tools. With assistance of Italian Government financing, SNEL has undertaken a
limited rehabilitation program at this plant, supervised by ENEL engineering
consultants. The Inga II plant, commissioned in 1982, has a 1,424 MW capacity
of which only 200 MW used for partial supply of Kinshasa, the mining industry in
Shaba and power exchanges with Zambia.  As uniits ar both power stations were
designed with respect to a much higher load forecast, their underutilization
resulted in destructive  cavitation phenomena.   The operating personnel has
limited itself to urgent and indispensable repairs and often has to use material
taken out from other units.   Three consultants (ICM, CIS and HydroQuebec)
carried out three separate inspections of the dam and power plants.   The
services of the first two were financed by SNEL, while those of HydroQuebec were
financed from the funds of CR 1224-ZR.  All three concluded that the state of
the plants required rehabilitation and managerial strengthening. Their reports
are included in the Project File (Annex 7.1).   The panel of experts from
HydroQuebec, reviewed all previous studies and inspection reports and carried
out a detailed diagnosis of the hydrofacilities and the Zairian Interconnected
System (Inga-Bas Zaire- Kinshasa a.c. power system/Inga-Shaba D.C. line/Shaba
a.c. power system). The parnel has strongly recommended: (i) limited but urgent
measures for civil works structures; (ii) complete rehabilitation of Inga I
units; (iii) addition of instruments and equipment to establish synchronous
operation of the Inga-Bas Za're-Kinshasa a.c. system with the Inga-Shaba d.c.
line; and (iv) rehabilitation and relay installation to initiate a load-shedding
program in the Shaba region which would minimize disturbances to GECAMINES. The
proposed project includes the supervision, procurement and installation of
equipment and spares responding to highest priority needs over the next eighteen
months. The remainder of works and comprehensive rehabilitation in both power
p'lants and the interconnected system would be taken up in the centext of the



88
ANNEX 4.1
Page 3 of 5
proposed energy project now being prepared. Tne total forecast of the emergency
repairs and operational Improvements are estimated at US$2.4 million out of
which US$2.0 million would be in foreign currency.
Radio-commmnication Equipment for SNEL
5.         The high frequency radio-communication equipment needed for improving
and strengthening the conmmunication channels between headquarters and principal
regional centers would be installed at Kinshasa, Inga (control center for the
Bas Zaire-Kinshasa-Shaba interconnected system), Lubumbashi (head office of the
Southern region), Bukavu (head office of the Kivu subregion) and Kisangani (head
office of the Eastern region of which Kivu is a subregion).  It is estimated
that the equipment would cost US$0.1 million of which US$90,000 would be in
foreign currency.
Consulting Engineering Services
6.            Consulting engineering services would cover the preparation of
detailed project and bid documents, evaluation of bids and construction
supervision for the rehabilitation and extension works. The total cost of
engineering services, including services carried out for the feasibility studies
is estimated to be US$3.0 million of which US$2.9 million would be in foreign
currency.
II. Technical and Institutional Assistance to SNEL
7.                   Within  the  Shaba  Power  System  Rehabilitation  Project
(CR  1224-ZR),   technical  and  institutional  assistance  is  provided   to
SNEL co strengthen accounting and management control systems, prepare a
long-term development plan, revalue assets and to carry out a tariff study.
SNEL's current management performance is much improved compared to 1981 when IDA
lending to SNEL was initiated, due in part to the intensive inputs by tile
consultants ard expatriate management staff.   Further technical assistance is
included in the proposed project for the following:
(a) continuation of the institutional general management assistance
particularly in personnel, financial, commercial and procurement
activities;
(b) continuation of the assistance in the establishment and training
of the planning and revisions in 1987 and !988 of the power
development plan and investment programs;
(c) Levisions of the assets revaluation studies;
(d) audits of SNEL's 1985, 1986 and 1987 accounts;
(e) an update of the 1984/85 tariff study;
(f) an audit of the operation of SIS and a study of the long-term
options of the SIS and tapping stations;



89
ANNEX 4.1
Page 4 of 5
(g) pre-feasibility   studies  for  those  projects   included  in  the
long-term power development plan;
(h) preparation of a contract for the transfer from the Government to
SNEL of ownership rights of assets financed and presently owned
by the Government but operated by SNEL;
(i) studies of urban household energy needs, supplies and feasibility
of various options (to be supervised by SNEL on behalf of the Ministry
of Mines and Energy); and,
(j) services of eight qualified experts for a period of two years starting
from July 1, 1986, as summarized below:
(i) one expert in power system planning to continue assisting
SNEL in the establishment and training of the planning unit;
(ii) four experts in finance to continue helping SNEL to strengthen
its commercial services and its finances (one financial advisor
in Kinshasa headquarters, one financial expert in Lubumbashi to
be responsible for the Southern region, one commercial expert in
the Kinshasa region and one financial expert in charge of
management of the SNEL's treasury);
(iii) two experts in organization (one expert in personnel management
and one expert in organization and data management services) to
help SNEL setting up a new organizational structure and a new
personnel management policy; and,
(iv) one expert in power transmission to help SNEL liaise with the
panel of experts for Inga I and Inga II hydro power facilities
a-d to review and implement its recommendations.
These experts will coordinate for SNEL the work of the general management
assistance consultants mentioned in (a) of this paragraph.   The total cost,
including  contingencies,  of  this  component  is  estimated  to  be  US$8.8
million of which US$8.1 million would be in foreign currency.
III. Training
8.           No technical training activities have been taking place for the
last three years at SNEL's training center at Sanga owing to the lack of
expertise to organize and operate the facilities.   Established in 1976, the
training center requires minimal rehabilitation, management support and
training of the twelve instructors.   SNEL needs to take the following short
and  medium  term  measures  in  order  to  ensure  the  proper  operation,
maintenance and management of its facilities by skilled staff: upgrading of
staff skills by advanced and on-the-job training, occupational adaptation,
occupational change,and acquisition and issuance of equipment and tools
required by operating units. Details are given in Annexes 4.2 and 7.1. SNEL



90
ANNEX 4.1
Page 5 of 5
has recently signed an agreement with EdF International for the services in
1986 and 1987 of a training consultant who will prepare and implement an
interim action plan for short-term (1986-1987)training needs. The services
of the consultant are financed from the CCCE loan which cofinances the
Shaba Power System Rehabilitation project with IDA (CR 1224-ZR) and
Belgium. Funds proposed under this project would cover part of the needs of
the interim action plan for training (to be prepared by July 31, 1986) and
would be used for:
(a) rehabilitation of existing facilities;
(b) installation of equipment for training in the operation and
maintenance of diesel plants;
(c) continuation in 1988 of the services of a senior training
consultant; and,
(d) advanced training of instructors at Sanga or abroad.
The total cost of this component, including contingencies, is estimated to
be US$0.8 million out of which US$0.7 million would be in foreign currency.
As the logical extension of the human resources activities is a longer term
training plan, the existing institutional assistance for human resources
would be reinforced to complete the Personnel Master Plan and develop from
it a Training Master Plan; to be completed and furnished to IDA for
comments, by November 1, 1986.

ZAIRE
SOCIETE NATIONALE D'ELECTRICITE (SNEL)
SECOND POWER PROJECT
Survey of Training Needs for SNEL
Situtation
1.        In 1976, SNEL established a Permanent Training Center at Sanga to
train its staff in management and technical aspects of electric power
operations.    Until  1980, the  Sanga Training  Center  benefitted  from
technical assistance financed by France.  Owing to the departure of the
French experts and limited access to foreign exchange for materials and
equipment, training activities declined steadily and came to a halt in
1983/84.  Resumption of training is a critical necessity in view of the
skills shortage which in certain fields makes SNEL reliant on non-national
personnel.
Improvements to the Sanga Training Center
2.            Minor rehabilitation (US$100,000) and equipment acquisition
(US$330,000), technical assistance for management of the center and
training for Zairian instructors (US$220,000) are required to return the
center to useful operation in the short-term and to eventually transfer
its management and operation to SNEL staff.
Skills Upgrading Program
3.            Target groups, representing priority categories for skills
strengthening, include supervisory personnel/managers, foremen and skilled
operating personnel.
- Supervisory Personnel.   Short-term advanced training would be
given to staff in the areas of accounting, financial management
budgetary  control,   internal  auditing,  and  operation  and
maintenance  of  electric  power  facilities.    Training  would
emphasize on the planning, organization and implementation of
work,  technical mastery and reporting.   Medium-term  training
would focus on new technology and methods.
- Foremen.     Intensive  on-the-job  training  is  proposed  for
(i) administration and accounting services staff in the subjects
of management, budgetary controls and customer relations and (ii)
technical services staff for hydroelectric plant maintenance,
network rehabilitation, construction of low voltage networks,
and HV-MV transformers station operation.   Particular attention
will be given to coordinating with programs underway at Ruzizi
and in Kinshasa, Goma, Bukavu, etc. Medium-term training would
focus on advanced operating techniques and development of
awareness of foremen's responsibilities.



93
ANNEX 4.2
Page 2 of 2
- Skilled Operating Personnel. Short-term training is proposed for
plant  maintenance  technicians,  electricians  and  transformer
station  operators  already  in  service.   Medium-term advanced
training is proposed in the areas listed above as well as for
staff involved in distribution network construction, maintenance
and operation, and administration and accounting.
Interim Action and Master Training Plans
4.        An interim action plan to identify inter a'ia specific skills to
be reinforced, programs and manpower needs for 1986/87, is currently being
identified by SNEL and Electricite de France (financed by the CCCE under
the Shaba Power Rehabilitation Project, Cr.1224-ZR) with target completion
scheduled for July 31, 1986 and implementation to begin by September 1,
1986. Physical works completion and equipment installation at Sanga would
be done during the 1986/87 scolar year. SNEL will have its consultants on
institutional assistance prepare until November 1, 1986, a Master Plan for
Training, based on SNEL's master plan for performance.

ZAIRE
SOCIETE NATIONALE D'ELECTRICITE (SNEL)
SECOND POWER PROJECT
PROJECT MONITORING SYSTEM
1. *Several factors affect the project implementation schedule and
the ultimate successful completion of the project. The key items to be
monitored would be as follows:
(a) Issue of tender documents: September 1, 1986
(b) Receipt of tenders: October 31, 1986.
(c) Contract awards: December 31, 1986.
(d) Construction progress:
- Completion of the rehabilitation of the first unit of
Ruzizi I: June 30, 1988
- Completion of the rehabilitation of Ruzizi I: December 31,
1989
- Completion of the feeder for interconnection to Ruzizi II
system: June 30, 1988
- Completion of the rehabilitation and extension of the Kivu
transmission and distribution network: December 31, 1988
(e) Progress on the technical assistance relative to the action
program agreed performance targets: variables to be monitored
will  be:  losses;  number  of  connections  per  employee;
receivables in months of sales; operating ratio; internal cash
generation contribution to construction expenditures.
(f) Progress on the training activities:
- Completion of the interim action program for training:
July 31, 1986
- Start of the training according to the interim action plan:
September 1, 1986
- Completion of the draft long term master plan for training:
November 1, 1986
(g) Annual review of SNEL's    financial status.



103
ANNEX 4.7
Page 2 of 2
(h) Comparison of the actual performance relative to appraisal
forecast and financial covenants; variables to be monitored
will be: financial rate of return, debt service ratio,
accumulated cash, debt and debt plus equity ratio, current
ratio, annual revaluation ratio.
(i) Consumer inventory/billing collection improvement program in
the Kivu region:
- Completion of the program: December 31, 1986
(j) Completion   of   the   long-term  power  development   plan:
September 30, 1986
(k) Completion of the systematic review of rehabilitation/
expansion/substitution needs of the diesel and isolated hydro
plants and associated networks: September 30, 1986
(1) Transfer from the Government to  SNEL   of ownership rights of
Inga  II  hydroelectric  plant,  Inga-Shaba  line  and  other
installations: June 30, 1987
(m) Final adjustment of SNEL's accounts in accordance to methods
satisfactory to IDA: December 31, 1987.

ZAIRE
SECOND POWER PROJECT
SOCIETE NATIONALE D 'ELECTRICITE
Notes and Assumptions for Financial Statements
A. INFLATION.   International and local inflation rates used in  the
financial projections are shown below:
1985  1986  1987  1988  1989  1990  1991  1992 thereafter
International     -    7.2Z  6.8%  6.8%  7.0Z  7.1%    4%   4%        4%
Local             30%   25%   20%   17%   15%   15%   12%   12%       12%
B. REVENUES.   Sales and other revenues will be equal to the sum of (i)
operating expenses, (ii) debt service, (iii) working capital needs and (iv)
a reasonable contribution to investment requirements.
Sales revenues are based on yearly rate increases averaging 23% for 1986
and 86%, 24Z, 22Z, 15%, 15Z and 14% for 1987 through 1992 respectively.
Other revenues represent interest earned on bank deposits, charges for
customer services and connection fees. Increases for the 1986-1992 period
primarily reflect the growth in new connections for programed distribution
expansion.   Based on 8,000 new  connections completed in  1985, it is
expected that SNEL can maintain that level for 1986 and sustain a program
which augments by 2,000 new connections annually.
C . OPERATING EXPENSES
(a)  Salaries.   Efforts to reduce costs and improve efficiency will
result in staff reduction from 4,700 in 1985 to a level of 4,500 in 1986,
which will remain constant throughout-the projected period. Salaries have
been adjusted to forecast inflation and an increase of 1% annually in real
terms to provide for upward mobility and merit remuneration.
(b)   Fuel.   (i)   No major investments in thermal generation are
programmed which would impact on fuel consumption.   Diesel fuel and
lubricant consdmption therefore reflects the expected longer operating
hours of thermal stations following Government approval of a negotiated
cost-based thermal tariff for extended operation of isolated plants and
actual operating norms of 0.30 1/kWh for diesel and 0.03 l/kWh for
lubricant.   (ii) The cost of gasoline for vehicle operation is based on
standards of 20 I/day for operational vehicles and 10 1/day for
administrative vehicles. Petroleum product costs are based on April 1986
prices (Z 26/1 for diesel and Z 30/1 for gasoline).   As prices were
decreased by 7% in April below their January 1986 levels, no further change
in prices is assumed for the remainder of 1986. From 1987 onwards, local
prices have been adjusted to compensate for the devaluation of the zaire
currency, assuming relative stability in the international traded price of
petroleum products.



108
ANNEX 5.4
Page 2 of 3
(c) Maintenance. Maintenance costs (primarily parts and materials for
facilities other than the Inga Shaba transmission line) are projected at 1%
of the gross value of revalued assets; an acceptable level to meet good
operating standards.
(d) Administrative Services and Supplies include general supplies and
services contracted from third parties, including maintenance of the
Inga-Shaba  line.    From  1987 onwards,  maintenance costs of  the line
(US$11 million equivalent in 1985) are expected to decrease by 19%
following a reduction in technical assistance through replacement bySNEL
staff.   All administrative costs are expected to increase in line with
local inflation from 1986 onwards.
(e)   Depreciation is charged at 3.0% of the value of revalued gross
fixed assets from 1986 onwards.
(f)  Taxes.  SNEL is not expected to pay corporate taxes due to its
substantial investment financing needs for which it will be solely
responsible beginning in 1987. Taxes for the projection period therefore
consist only of a statistical tax of 3% of the value of imported goods plus
entry duty on all non-eletrical materials.   Together, these charges are
estimated at a level of 3% of the foreign exchange cost of the construction
program and equipment renewal.
(g)   Interest expenses have been projected based on the terms of
existing debt and forecast interest of 8.5%.
D. BALANCE SHEET
(a)     Fixed  Assets  in  operation  were  revalued  by  consultants
(para. 5.04) as of December 31, 1985 on the basis of their utility value
(replacement value adjusted for exchange variation, wear and utilisable
capacity).    Fixed  assets  for  the  projection  period  (1986-92)  were
determined by (i) the transfer of completed construction and renewed
equipment in accordance with -SNEL's investment program (ii) annual
revaluation in accordance with international inflation (for 80% of the
asset base comprised of foreign pucchased goods or services) and local
price escalation (for 20% of the base representing the local input).
The transfer of the Inga II hydroelectric facilties to SNEL's
accounts has been related to the capacity utilization of the plant, e.g.
30% of the value of the plant was transferred in 1985, an additional 10% in
1988 and  1991,  respectively.    The Government  of Zaire  financed  the
construction of the facilities with a loan of US$380 million.  With the
gradual transfer of Inga II to SNEL's accounts, it has also been assumed
that SNEL will repay the original value of this loan: 30% of the loan is
shown in SNEL's accounts as a liability in 1985, 40% in 1988 and 50X in
1991 less repayments which are assumed to commence in 1987 over a ten year
period. The remaining value of the plant has been transferred to SNEL as
Government equity.



109
ANNEX 5.4
Page 3 of 3
(b)  Other  Assets  include  intangible  assets,  personnel  loans  and
advances on works in progress.   Because of the low degree of accurate
predictability for these values, they are assumed to remain constant.
(c)  Accounts Receivable are estimated to decline to a level of four
months' sales in 1986, three months' sales in 1987 and 2.5 months' sales in
is88 and thereafter.
(d) Inventory represents materials and supplies for plant operation and
maintenance estimated at about 1% of the gross revalued assets under normal
operation.
(e)   Other Current Assets is comprised of advances and credits for
maintenance and service contracts, prepaid expenses and social security,
and miscellaneous deposits.  A substantial decrease is expected for 1986
owing to new contracting arrangements for maintenance beginning in 1987.
Tne future value of these items is expected to remain at 20% of total
maintenance, contracted administrative services and personnel costs.
(f) Equipment Subsidy was eliminated in 1986 with the
expiration of bilateral agreements and the adoption of a policy of 100%
self-financing by SNEL (i.e. end of Government subsidization).
(g)  Accounts payable are projected at a level of 25% of contracted
fuel, administrative and consumable maintenance items.
(h) Advances and deposits pertain to consumer deposits for connections
and are projected at 3OZ of other revenues (generated from connection
charges).
(i)    Other  Creditors  and  Banks  includes  short-term  notes  and
miscellaneous accrued expenses. Short-term notes were liquidated in 1985.
(j) Other comprises unpaid taxes and charges due to Government.
E.   DEBT.   Long-term debt is expected to be obtained for the foreign
exchange cost of SNEL's 1986-92 investment program loans, credits and
grants to the Government and on-lent to SNEL at 8.5% interest. The loans
are assumed to be repayable in 20 years including three to five years
grace, depending on the construction period of the financed works, with
equal installments of princiapl.
F.  CASH.  Cash required for operations is assumed equal to one month's
operating expenses plus three months' debt service.  Surplus cash would
be used to establish a reserve for the short-term portion of long-term
deb;. pay dividends to the Government, or increase SNEL's contribution to
its investment program.

SOCIETE NATIONALE D'ELECTRICITE (SNEL)
SECOND POWER PROJFCT
Economic Rate of Return Calculations for the Regional Investment Program
Assumptions: Project lives: 30 years
Demand forecasts:   Burundi: SAR for Power I Transmission and Distribution
Project (Report No. 5312-BU);   Rwanda: SAR for Power Project (Report No.
4961-RW); Zaire (para. 2.25 of this report); estimates giving for the region an
annual increase of 10% over the 1984-1995 period.
Sales of energy from Rwegura and Ruzizi II begin in 1988 and reach US$224 GWh
corresponding to full utilization of the facilities in 1995 (see page 3 of this
annex).
1.   Calculation of Benefits
A.   Without taking Into account consumers' surplus:
Resulting sales in Burundi, Rwanda and Zaire (Kivu) multiplied by the respective
forecast retail tariffs.
B.  Taking into account consumers' surplus:
The consumers in the region whose consumption represents about 15Z of the total
demand from Ruzizi II and Rwegura would be ready in case the Rwegura and Ruzizi
II projects were not built, to buy their electricity from a diesel-fueled
generating station, at a cost of at least UStl9.2/kWh.   1/   Either these
consumers would have an urgent need for electricity (hospital, water pumping,
Government agencies) or their demand is very inelastic (industries for which
electricity is a small part of their cost and which can pass the increase in
electricity cost to their customers, expatriates whose living expenses are paid
for, etc).
SDecific consumption of diesel oil: 0.320 kg/kWh 2/. Estimated average economic
cost of diesel oil in the region in April 1986: USt51/liter (US$600/tonne).
1/ SAR for the Ruzizi II Regional Hydroelectric Power Project, May 1983; Report
No. 4303-EAF.
2/ SAR for the Burundi Power Transmission and Distribution Project, Report
No. 5312-BU, February 1985; and Burundi - Review of Petroleum Import and
Distribution Arrangement, ESMAP, January 1984.

through forecast electricity retail tariffs. The demand curve for the region
could be drawn with two points (100% consumption at the average tariff, and 15%
at diesel cost), and a consumers' surplus could be calculated  that would
increase the benefits of the project.

The revenue of the sale of Ruzizi II and Rwegura electricity is represented by
the area abcd, the value of which is TxQ. The theoretical surplus of consumers
ready to buy electricity up to the diesel cost D is represented by the area
above the tariff line limited by the demand curve:   ebcf.  To simplify the
calculation, we assume that the f4c curve is a straight line, thus the value of
the consumers' surplus is: 0.15(D-T)%? -- I 0.85(%D-T)Q - 0.575(D-T)Q.
With total incremental costs C and revenues TQ and the above
consumers' surplus, the economic rate of return is the discount rate at which
there is equilibrium between C and TQ + 0.575(D-T)Q - (0.425T + 0.575D)Q.

ANNEX 6.2
Page 1 of 2
ZAIRE
SOCIETE NATIONALE D'ELECTRICITE (SNEL)
SECOND POWER PROJECT
Basis for Calculation of Economic Rate of Return
of the Rehabilitation of the Ruzizi I Hydropower Plant
1.        The economic rate of return was computed as the discount rate at
which the present economic value of the project equals the present value of
savings to be obtained in not building the alternative of the project.
2.          The capital costs of the rehabilitation, spare parts and new
equipment (exclusive of the technical assistance and training components)
and of the alternative option have been estimated at March 1986 prices.
3.           The benefits are assumed  to be the savings from a major
replacement (the alternative project) which would be needed in the future
in case the rehabilitation is not carried out in 1986-1989.   In such a
case, -t is estimated that the station would need to be completely replaced
by 1992.
4.        Interest during construction and taxes were not included.
5.        The streams of costs and revenues are presented in the table on
page 2 of this annex.  The economic rate of return is calculated as 21.1%.
Results of the sensitiviy studies are stmmarized as follows:
Project Costs     Project Costs
The Economic Rates of Return (Z)    as Estimated         up by 10Z
Load growth in the regional
interconnected system as estimated
(9.4Z p.a.)                               21.1               17.5
Load growth 8.2Z p.a.                     15.9               13.2
Load growth 7.2Z p.a.                     12.7               10.6
In all cases the economic rates of return are acceptable.
6.         As shown in the table given in page 2 of this Annex, if the
rehabilitation project is not carried out in 1986-1989, then in 1991 a
deficit in the interconnected system would occur.   To make up for this
deficit, either the alternative project would be advanced to end-1991 or
some of the consumers would utilize their diesel units (it is estimated
that 15Z of the consumers would have their small independent diesel
units). As in both cases their effects would be to increase the ERR, these
sensitivity runs have not been done.

SECOND POWER PROJECT
Basis for Calculation of Economic Rate of Return for the Rehabilitation and
Extension of the Distribution Networks in the Kivu Region
1.          The economic rate of return was determined as the discount rate at
which the present values of all incremental capital and operating costs and the
benefits and revenues attributable to the rehabilitation and development, are
equal. It is estimated that without rehabilitation, the distribution capacity
would decrease by about 5Z per year in 1987-1991 period.
Assumptions
2.        Project Life: 30 years
Costs as based on constant March 1986 prices.
Interest during construction and taxes not included.
Connection costs are paid by consumers as consumers' contributions
Cost of energy is taken from Annex 6.4: marginal cost of generation of
energy: USt 6.2/kWh sent out.
Losses assumed to be 13Z.
Operation and maintenance costs: 7Z of the investments.
Retail tarifs for Kivu assumed as the long run marginal cost for Kivu,
from 1988.
Benefits
Benefits were calculated with respect to electicity tariffs.
Sensitivity Studies
Sensitivity studies were carried out using different annual growth
rates for the expected sales and a project cost increase of 10Z. Results are
summarized in the following table:
Project Costs          Project Costs
Economic Rate of Return (Z)         as Estimated             up by 10l
Load growth in the Kivu Region
of 8.1% (base case)                   15.6                     14.4
Load growth in the Kivu Region
of 7.1%                               14.9                     13.7

Consumers' Surplus
Many consumers in the region would have used their existing
diesel units and would have installed new ones in the case the distribution
network is not rehabiliated and strengthened.  Consultants (ICM) identified
the diesel units existing in the Goma-Bukavu-Uvira-Kiliba area.  ji As
their effects would be to increase the ERR, these sensitivity runs have not
been done.
1/ Sugar mill in Kiliba: 250 kVA and 140 kVA
Economat general: 30 kVA and 40 kVA
Estagrico (cotton factory) 125 kVA and 160 kVA
2 hotels: 2 x 15 kVA
Katana hospital: 60 kVA
Agriculture research center in Inera (Katana): 41 kVA
3 tea factories: 3 x 60 kVA
Total about 1,056 kVA; (850 kW).
Average annual operation period: 2,000 h (load factor of 23%)
Specific consumption: 0.4 kg/kWh sent out

Marginal Transmission and Distribution Costs
4.        The proposed project would need to be complemented by 15 kV or
6.6 kV/0.4 kV substation investments which are estimated to be about US$0.7
million annually in 1990 and decreasing to US$0.4 million in 1994. Life of the
project: 30 years; operation and maintenance costs: 7% of the investment costs.
Without rehabilitation the distribution network's capacity would decrease by
about 5% per year between 1987-1991.  The proposed project would provide for
rehabilitation and extension of the distribution networks; the transmission
lines do not require extensive rehabilitation nor extension.

SOCIETE NATIONALE D'ELECTRICITE
SECOND POWER PROJECT
Economic Rate of Return for the
Shaba Power System Rehabilitation Project
1.        The economic rate of return was determined as 16Z in the Staff
Appraisal Report No. 3400a-ZR dated March 15, 1982. As costs and tariffs
have changed since that date, the rate of return was recomputed.
2.        The economic rate of return is the discount rate at which the
present economic value of the project and operating costs equals the
present value of savings and revenues expected to be obtained from the
project.
3.        The capital cost of the repairs, spare parts and new equipment
exclusive of the technical assistance and training components are estimated
at March 1986 prices. The economic life of the project was assumed to be
15 years from the date it is completed.
4.        The benefits are assumed to be revenues from the sale of
electricity which is available due to the rehabilitation program. It is
assumed that without rehabilitation the output of the system would fall
from 2700 GWh in 1982 per year to 1500 GWh in 1994 at about 100 GWh
annually, and then remain constant at this value. 1982 was the year just
before the Inga-Shaba d.c. line was fully commissioned and the power plants
in Shaba had practically to supply all the dem-nd in the region. The power
transmitted through SIS is not firm and the Shaba power plants are
rehabilitated to provide firm power and energy for the region and to
GECAMINES. The average tariff in the Shaba region was calculated as
UScO.487/kWh.
5.        It was assumed that this rehabilitation project does not require
an increase in operating and maintenance costs.
6.        The streams of costs and revenues are presented in the table on
page 2 of this annex. The economic rate of return is estimated to be about
12 Z.
7.        Revised base costs and physical contingencies for the project are
as follow:
